Ruling on Marrying Sabian Women
Ruling on marrying Sabians with conditions from the Hanafi madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Muslim man may marry a Sabian woman according to the Hanafi madhhab and distinguish between permissible and impermissible scenarios.

Core Idea
Marrying a Sabian woman is permissible only if she believes in a prophet and has a divine book; star worship without a book makes her a polytheist.

This passage addresses the ruling on marrying Sabian women, a group whose religious identity is ambiguous. The Hanafi school makes permissibility conditional on their being considered People of the Book. This lesson unpacks the conditions from al-Hidayah and the correct position according to later authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is marrying a Sabian woman. The text begins by stating the ruling is not unrestricted; it is conditional.
2
Determine the conditions from al-Hidayah
The permissibility is restricted by two conditions: belief in a prophet and acknowledgment of a divine book. If either is missing, the woman is considered a polytheist.
3
Understand the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf on star worship
Some early authorities held that star worship does not negate kitabi status as long as a book exists, but the correct view (mufta bihi) is that actual star worship makes them polytheists.
The difference arises from confusion about the actual beliefs of the Sabians.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The operative ruling: marriage is permitted only if the Sabian woman has a prophet and book AND does not engage in actual star worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والصابئة) أي وحل تزوجها أطلقه وقيده في الهداية بقوله : إن كانوا يؤمنون بدين نبي ويقرون بكتاب الله لأنهم من أهل الكتاب وإن كانوا يعبدون الكواكب ولا كتاب لهم لم تجز مناكحتهم لأنهم مشركون والخلاف المنقول فيه محمول على اشتباه مذهبهم فكل أجاب على ما وقع عنده وعلى هذا حل ذبيحتهم ا هـ . وصححه أيضاً في غاية البيان وغيره أنه لا خلاف بينهم في الحقيقة لكن ظاهر الهداية أن منع مناكحتهم مقيد بقيدين : عبادة الكواكب وعدم الكتاب. فلو كانوا يعبدون الكواكب ولهم كتاب تجوز مناكحتهم وهو قول بعض المشايخ زعموا أن عبادة الكواكب لا تخرجهم عن كونهم أهل الكتاب، والصحيح أنهم إن كانوا يعبدونها حقيقة فليسوا أهل كتاب، وإن كانوا يعظمونها كتعظيم المسلمين للكعبة فهم لم يدعه والكل منتف ولا يخفى ما في عدم عدها خامسة ونحوه من عدم الاحتياط في وقوعه في من المحرم .
His statement: 'And the Sabians' - meaning the permissibility of marrying them. He made it unrestricted but restricted it in al-Hidayah by saying: If they believe in the religion of a prophet and acknowledge a book of Allah, then they are from the People of the Book. But if they worship the stars and have no book, then marrying them is not permissible because they are polytheists. The difference of opinion transmitted about them is attributed to confusion about their sect; each answered according to what occurred to him. On this basis, their slaughtered meat is permissible. And it is also affirmed in Ghayat al-Bayan and others that there is no real difference among them, but the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that the prohibition of marrying them is restricted by two conditions: worship of stars and lack of a book. So if they worship the stars but have a book, marrying them is permissible. This is the view of some mashayikh who claim that star worship does not remove them from being People of the Book. The correct view is that if they actually worship them, they are not People of the Book. If they venerate them like Muslims venerate the Kaaba, then they do not claim otherwise. And all this is negated, and it is not hidden what is in not counting it a fifth and the like of lack of caution in falling into the prohibited.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on marrying Sabian women. The author explains that the ruling is conditional. According to al-Hidayah, if the Sabians believe in a prophet and have a divine book, they are considered People of the Book, and marrying them is permissible. However, if they worship the stars and have no book, they are polytheists and marriage is not permissible. The differing opinions among scholars about Sabians are due to confusion about their actual beliefs. Some scholars hold that even if they worship the stars, as long as they have a book, they are still People of the Book. The correct view is that actual star worship negates being a kitabi, but if they only venerate the stars as Muslims venerate the Kaaba, they may still be considered kitabi. The passage ends with a caution that not classifying them as a fifth category (i.e., disbelievers) may lead to falling into prohibition.

Key Terms
صابئة (sabi'ah): A Sabian; a religious group that may worship stars and claim a prophet and book.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, when is it permissible for a Muslim man to marry a Sabian woman?
A) Always permissible regardless of beliefs.
B) Only if she converts to Islam.
C) If she believes in a prophet and has a divine book.
D) Only if she does not worship the stars.

Key Takeaway
Marriage to a Sabian woman is permissible only if she believes in a prophet and has a divine book; actual star worship makes her a polytheist and marriage impermissible.

Marriage to a Free Kitabiyyah: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on free Kitabiyyah with proof and karahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a free Kitabiyyah woman and explain the reason for its dislike for a harbiyyah Kitabiyyah.

Core Idea
Marrying a free Kitabiyyah is permissible, but disliked for a harbiyyah due to harmful influence on children.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi ruling on marrying a free woman from the People of the Book. The author confirms the basic permissibility based on the Qur'an and agreement of the four Imams, then raises a practical caution: marriage to a Kitabiyyah from dar al-harb is disliked (makruh tanzihan) because of the risk that children raised in such an environment may adopt the traits of the enemy. This lesson unpacks the textual evidence and the reason for the karahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Marrying a free Kitabiyyah woman. The ruling is mubah (permissible) overall, but for a harbiyyah it is makruh tanzihan.
Note: The karahah is tanzihi, not tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Qur'anic verses: Yunus 10:18, an-Nisa 4:116, and al-Baqarah 2:221 to establish that the prohibition of marrying polytheists applies only to idolaters, excluding People of the Book.
The verses mention shirk, but the author interprets 'mushrik' as idolater specifically.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The term 'mushrik' in the prohibition is interpreted in its specific meaning of 'worshipper of idols', not including Jews and Christians.
This is based on the linguistic principle of specifying a general term (amm) by context.
4
Note the karahah
Why is marriage to a harbiyyah Kitabiyyah disliked? Because of the risk that children will adopt the manners of the people of war, as stated in al-Muhit.
The dislike is tanzihi because there is no explicit prohibition.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: permissible (mubah) but better to avoid unless necessary. For harbiyyah, it is makruh tanzihan.
This is the position of the later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكتابية والصابئة والمحرمة ولو محرماً والأمة ولو كتابية والحرة على الأمة لا عكسه ولو
اه.
والظاهر
عما يشركون﴾ [يونس : ۱۸] المراد مطلق الشرك، وكذا في قوله تعالى إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ﴾ [النساء : ١١٦] فيتناول جميع الكفار. وفي قوله ولا تنحكوا المشركات) [البقرة : ۲۲۱] المراد به المشرك ظاهراً وباطناً وهو الوثني فلا يتناول أهل الكتاب والمنافقين وأطلقه أيضاً فشمل الكتابية الحرة والأئمة، واتفق الأئمة الأربعة على حل الحرة واختلفوا في حل الأمة كما سيأتي هذا والأولى أن لا يتزوج كتابية ولا يأكل ذبائحهم إلا لضرورة. وفي المحيط : يكره تزوج الكتابية الحربية لأن الإنسان لا يأمن أن يكون بينهما ولد فينشأ على طبائع أهل الحرب ويتخلق بأخلاقهم فلا يستطيع المسلم قلعه عن تلك العادة ا هـ . أنها كراهة تنزية لأن التحريمية لا بد لها من نهي أو ما في معناه لأنها في رتبة الواجب. وفي الخانية : تزوج الحربية مكروه فإن خرج بها إلى دار الإسلام بقي النكاح ا هـ . وأشار المصنف إلى أنه يحل وطء الكتابية بملك اليمين وسيأتي أن الكتابية إذا تمجست فإنه ينفسخ نكاحها من المسلم بخلاف اليهودية إذا تنصرت أو عكسه. وذكر الأسبيجابي أن للمسلم منع الذمية إذا تزوجها من الخروج إلى الكنائس والبيع وليس له إجبارها على الغسل من الحيض والجناية. وفي الخانية من فصل الجزية من السير: مسلم له امرأة ذمية ليس له أن يمنعها من شرب الخمر لأن شرب الخمر حلال عندها وله أن يمنعها عن اتخاذ الخمر في المنزل ا هـ. وهو مشكل لأنه وإن كان حلالاً عندها لكن رائحتها تضره فله منعها كمنع المسلمة من أكل الثوم والبصل ولذا قال الكركي في الفيض قبيل باب التيمم : إن المسلم له أن يمنع زوجته الذمية من شرب الخمر كالمسلمة لو أكلت الثوم والبصل وكان زوجها يكره ذلك له أن يمنعها ا هـ .
وهذا هو
الحق كما لا يخفى.
The Kitabiyyah and the Sabi'ah and the woman in ihram even if the husband is in ihram, and the slave woman even if Kitabiyyah, and the free woman over the slave woman not the opposite, etc. The apparent meaning... [Yunus:18] intends absolute polytheism, likewise in His saying 'Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [an-Nisa:116] so it includes all disbelievers. And in His saying 'And do not marry polytheistic women' [al-Baqarah:221] intends the polytheist in outward and inner, i.e., the idolater, so it does not include the People of the Book and hypocrites, and it is absolute so it includes the free Kitabiyyah and the slave. The four Imams agreed on the permissibility of the free [woman] and differed on the permissibility of the slave as will come. This, and it is better not to marry a Kitabiyyah nor eat their slaughter except for necessity. In al-Muhit: It is disliked to marry a Kitabiyyah harbiyyah because one cannot be safe from having a child between them who grows up upon the nature of the people of war and acquires their character, so the Muslim cannot uproot him from that habit. It is a tanzih dislike because prohibition necessarily requires a prohibition or something in its meaning since it is in the rank of obligatory. In al-Khaniyyah: Marrying a harbiyyah is disliked, then if he takes her out to Dar al-Islam the marriage remains. The author indicated that it is permissible to have intercourse with a Kitabiyyah by right hand possession, and it will come that if the Kitabiyyah becomes Magian then her marriage from the Muslim is annulled, unlike if the Jewess becomes Christian or vice versa. al-Asbijabi mentioned that the Muslim may prevent the dhimmi wife, if he marries her, from going out to churches and synagogues, but he cannot force her to perform ghusl from menstruation and janabah. In al-Khaniyyah from the chapter of jizya from al-Siyar: A Muslim has a dhimmi wife; he may not prevent her from drinking wine because wine is permissible according to her, but he may prevent her from making wine in the home. This is problematic because although it is permissible according to her, its smell harms him, so he may prevent her just as a Muslim woman may be prevented from eating garlic and onions. Hence al-Kirki said in al-Fayd prior to the chapter of tayammum: The Muslim may prevent his dhimmi wife from drinking wine just as if a Muslim woman ate garlic and onions and her husband disliked that, he may prevent her. This is the truth, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Qur'an. It first states the permissibility of marrying a free Kitabiyyah, citing the Qur'anic verse that restricts the prohibition of marrying polytheist women to idolaters, excluding People of the Book. The four Imams agree on this. However, the author notes that it is better to avoid marrying a Kitabiyyah unless necessary. The dislike is tanzihi (not tahrimi) for a harbiyyah Kitabiyyah due to potential negative influence on children. The passage then discusses the extent of the husband's authority over a dhimmi wife: he can prevent her from going to churches and from making wine at home, but not from drinking wine itself, although there is a conflicting view that he may prevent drinking if it harms him by smell. The Hanafi position is that the husband may prevent anything that harms him, analogous to preventing a Muslim wife from eating garlic.

Key Terms
الكتابية (al-kitabiyyah): A woman from the People of the Book (Jews or Christians).
الحربية (al-harbiyyah): A Kitabiyyah from a land at war with Muslims (dar al-harb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is marrying a harbiyyah Kitabiyyah disliked?
A) It is prohibited by the Qur'an.
B) Because of potential harm to the children's religious upbringing.
C) Because she cannot perform ghusl.
D) Because the husband cannot prevent her from drinking wine.

Key Takeaway
Marrying a free Kitabiyyah is permissible, but marrying a harbiyyah Kitabiyyah is disliked (makruh tanzihan) due to concern for children's development.

Husband's Authority Over His Dhimmi Wife
Husband's authority over dhimmi wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the extent of a husband's authority over his dhimmi wife regarding church attendance and wine consumption, and resolve the apparent conflict about preventing wine drinking.

Core Idea
A husband may prevent his dhimmi wife from acts that cause him harm, even if those acts are permissible in her religion.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical question: what authority does a Muslim husband have over his Kitabiyyah wife in matters of her religious practice? The author discusses whether he can prevent her from going to church, making wine, or drinking wine. While some early texts say he cannot prevent drinking because it is permissible in her religion, al-Kirki argues that he can if the smell harms him, drawing an analogy to preventing a Muslim wife from eating garlic. This lesson resolves the conflict and establishes the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The husband may prevent his wife from acts that cause him harm. This is established by the analogy to preventing a Muslim wife from garlic.
The principle is rooted in the concept of harm (darar).
2
Examine the exception
Some Hanafi texts (al-Khaniyyah) state that the husband cannot prevent his dhimmi wife from drinking wine because it is halal for her.
This is based on her religious freedom.
3
Analyze the counterargument
Al-Kirki argues that the husband can prevent drinking if the smell harms him, analogizing to a Muslim wife's garlic.
The harm principle overrides the permissibility of her act.
4
Resolve the conflict
The author of Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with al-Kirki and declares his view as the truth.
This is the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply to other cases
The husband may also prevent her from going to church and from making wine at home, as these can be causes of harm or disturbance.
Preventing church is allowed per al-Asbijabi, and preventing making wine is allowed per al-Khaniyyah.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Qur'an. It first states the permissibility of marrying a free Kitabiyyah, citing the Qur'anic verse that restricts the prohibition of marrying polytheist women to idolaters, excluding People of the Book. Key Terms
الكتابية (al-kitabiyyah): A woman from the People of the Book (Jews or Christians).
الذمية (al-dhimmiyyah): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state (dhimmi), here a dhimmi wife.
الكركي (al-Kirki): A later Hanafi scholar (d. 922 AH) known for his work al-Fayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what action may a Muslim husband prevent his dhimmi wife from doing, according to the view of al-Asbijabi?
A) Drinking wine.
B) Going to church.
C) Making wine at home.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The husband's authority over his dhimmi wife extends to preventing acts that cause him direct harm, such as the smell of wine, but not to acts that are purely her religious practice without harm.

Marriage to Sabians: Conditions and Definition
Defining Sabians and marriage conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for marriage to a Sabian woman and identify the key factors that determine whether Sabians are considered Ahl al-Kitab.

Core Idea
Sabians are only eligible for marriage if they believe in a prophet and possess a scripture; star-worship negates this status.

The ruling on marrying Sabians appears absolute in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, but al-Hidayah and later authorities add critical restrictions. In this lesson, we will parse the conditions that determine whether a Sabian woman is considered from the People of the Book, and understand the dispute within the Hanafi school. The key is distinguishing between Sabians who worship stars and those who merely venerate celestial bodies while maintaining belief in a prophet and a book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its apparent scope
Start with the matn's statement: 'and the Sabi'ah' which implies that marriage to a Sabian woman is permissible absolutely. Note that this is the starting point before the restriction.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq; the commentary expands.
2
Locate the restriction from al-Hidayah
Read the condition: 'If they believe in the religion of a prophet and acknowledge a book of Allah, they are from the People of the Book'. This restricts permissibility to those Sabians who have faith in a prophet and a revealed scripture.
Those who worship stars and have no book are polytheists, so marriage is forbidden.
3
Distinguish between types of Sabians
Parse the two-part condition: (1) they must actually believe in a prophet and have a book; (2) if they worship stars but still have a book, some scholars differ. The correct view is that if they truly worship stars, they are not Ahl al-Kitab, but if they merely venerate stars like Muslims venerate the Ka'ba, they remain Ahl al-Kitab.
This distinction resolves the apparent confusion about their school.
4
Understand the intra-madhhab difference
Note that some mashayikh held that star-worship does not remove them from Ahl al-Kitab as long as they have a book. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that actual star-worship makes them polytheists, as confirmed by al-Hidayah and Ghayat al-Bayan.
The difference is attributed to confusion about the Sabians' actual beliefs.
5
Apply the ruling and determine the final position
Conclude: marriage to a Sabian woman is mubah (permissible) only if she is from the People of the Book, i.e., believes in a prophet and has a divinely revealed book. If she does not meet these conditions, marriage is haram because she is a polytheist.
The ruling also applies to the permissibility of their dhabihah (slaughtered meat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والصابئة) أي وحل تزوجها أطلقه وقيده في الهداية بقوله : إن كانوا يؤمنون بدين نبي ويقرون بكتاب الله لأنهم من أهل الكتاب وإن كانوا يعبدون الكواكب ولا كتاب لهم لم تجز مناكحتهم لأنهم مشركون والخلاف المنقول فيه محمول على اشتباه مذهبهم فكل أجاب على ما وقع عنده وعلى هذا حل ذبيحتهم ا هـ . وصححه أيضاً في غاية البيان وغيره أنه لا خلاف بينهم في الحقيقة لكن ظاهر الهداية أن منع مناكحتهم مقيد بقيدين : عبادة الكواكب وعدم الكتاب. فلو كانوا يعبدون الكواكب ولهم كتاب تجوز مناكحتهم وهو قول بعض المشايخ زعموا أن عبادة الكواكب لا تخرجهم عن كونهم أهل الكتاب، والصحيح أنهم إن كانوا يعبدونها حقيقة فليسوا أهل كتاب، وإن كانوا يعظمونها كتعظيم المسلمين للكعبة فهم لم يدعه والكل منتف ولا يخفى ما في عدم عدها خامسة ونحوه من عدم الاحتياط في وقوعه في
من
أهل كتاب كذا في المجتبى. وفي الكشاف : إنهم قوم عدلوا عن دين اليهودية والنصرانية وعبدوا الملائكة من صبأ إذا خرج من الدين . (1),
His statement: '(and the Sabi'ah)' i.e., the permissibility of marrying her is absolute, but al-Hidayah restricted it by saying: If they believe in the religion of a prophet and acknowledge a book of Allah, they are from the People of the Book; if they worship the stars and have no book, their marriage is not permissible because they are polytheists. The reported difference is attributed to confusion about their school, so each responded according to what occurred to him, and accordingly the permissibility of their slaughter. And he also corrected in Ghayat al-Bayan and others that there is no real difference among them, but the apparent meaning of al-Hidayah is that the prohibition of their marriage is restricted by two conditions: worship of stars and lack of a book. If they worship stars and have a book, their marriage is permissible—this is the view of some mashayikh who claim that worship of stars does not remove them from being People of the Book. The correct view is that if they actually worship them, they are not People of the Book; if they venerate them as Muslims venerate the Ka'ba, they have not left [the faith] and all is negated. It is not hidden what is in not counting them as fifth etc. of lack of precaution in falling into [error]. Thus in al-Mujtaba. And in al-Kashshaf: They are a people who deviated from the religion of Judaism and Christianity and worshipped angels; from 'ṣaba'a' meaning he left the religion.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines the Sabians and the conditions for marriage to them. The author explains that the ruling is absolute in the matn, but al-Hidayah restricts it: if Sabians believe in a prophet and have a scripture, they are considered People of the Book, and marriage is permissible. If they worship stars and have no book, they are polytheists and marriage is forbidden. There is a difference of opinion when they worship stars but still have a book. Some scholars say worship of stars does not negate their being People of the Book, but the correct view is that if they truly worship stars, they are not. However, if they merely venerate them, like Muslims venerate the Ka'ba, they remain People of the Book. The passage also notes that their slaughter (dhabihah) is permissible if they are considered People of the Book.

Key Terms
الصابئة (al-Ṣābi'ah): A religious group that deviated from Judaism and Christianity; some worship stars or angels.
الهداية (al-Hidāyah): The renowned Hanafi fiqh work by al-Marghinani that restricts the ruling on Sabians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) She must not worship stars at all.
B) She must believe in a prophet and have a divine book.
C) She must have a book, even if she worships stars.
D) She must come from a land at peace with Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Marriage to a Sabian woman is permissible only if she believes in a prophet and has a revealed scripture, and she does not actually worship stars as deities.

Marriage to a Kitabiyyah Slave Woman: The Hanafi vs Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i on marriage to a Kitabiyyah slave woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits marrying a Kitabiyyah slave woman, apply the Hanafi usul principle that a description (wasf) does not negate the ruling when absent, and compare the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A description (wasf) does not negate; the general command to marry good women includes Kitabiyyah slaves.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi position on marrying a slave woman who is a Kitabiyyah (Jew or Christian). The dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i hinges on a subtle usul point: whether the description 'believing' in the Quranic verse restricting slave women is a condition (shart) that excludes non-believers, or merely a description (wasf) that does not negate permissibility. Understanding this distinction is key to mastering Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school permits a free man to marry a Kitabiyyah slave woman. Identify the ruling from the text: 'والأمة ولو كتابية أي حل تزوجها'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i holds that the verse (an-Nisa:25) restricts marriage of slave women to 'believing' girls, so non-Muslim slaves are excluded. Note the phrase 'خلافاً للشافعي'.
3
Examine the Shafi'i proof
The opponent argues that the word 'believing' is a condition (shart) that negates the ruling when absent. This is based on the usul concept of mafhum al-shart.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis classify 'believing' as a description (wasf), not a condition. According to Hanafi usul, a description does not imply negation of the ruling when absent. Thus, the general command 'Marry what is good for you of women' (an-Nisa:3) applies to Kitabiyyah slave women.
The text says 'والخلاف مبني على مسألة أصولية هي أن مفهوم الشرط والوصف هل يكون معتبراً...'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafis permit marriage to a Kitabiyyah slave woman; Shafi'is require her to be Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأمة ولو كتابية أي حل تزوجها خلافاً للشافعي، وأصله التقييد بالوصف والشرط في قوله تعالى ومن لم يستطع منكم طولا أن ينكح المحصنات المؤمنات فما ملكت أيمانكم من فتياتكم المؤمنات ) [النساء : ٢٥] والخلاف مبني على مسألة أصولية هي أن مفهوم الشرط والوصف هل يكون معتبراً ينتفي الحكم بانتفائه ؟ فقال الشافعي : نعم. وقلنا : لا فصار الحل ثابتاً فيها بالعمومات مثل قوله فانكحوا ما طاب لكم من النساء﴾ [النساء:
His statement: '(and the slave woman even if Kitabiyyah)' i.e., the permissibility of marrying her, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Its basis is the restriction by description and condition in His statement: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then [marry] from those whom your right hands possess of your believing slave girls' [an-Nisa 4:25]. The dispute is based on an usul issue: whether the concept of condition and description is considered so that the ruling is negated by its negation. Al-Shafi'i said: Yes, and we said: No. Hence the permissibility is established for her by the generalities such as His statement: 'Marry what is good for you of women' [an-Nisa:...]
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is. The issue is whether a free man may marry a slave woman who is not a Muslim (Kitabiyyah). The Shafi'i school holds that the verse restricts marriage of slave women to 'believing' slave girls, so a non-Muslim slave woman is not permissible. The Hanafi school argues that the word 'believing' is a description (wasf) not a condition (shart), and according to Hanafi usul, a description does not negate the ruling when absent. Instead, the general command 'Marry what is good for you of women' includes Kitabiyyah slave women. Therefore, Hanafis permit marriage to a Kitabiyyah slave woman, while Shafi'is require her to be Muslim.

Key Terms
مفهوم الشرط والوصف (mafhūm al-sharṭ wa al-waṣf): The usul concept: whether a condition or description in a verse implies the negation of the ruling when it is absent.
المحصنات المؤمنات (al-muḥṣanāt al-mu'mināt): Free believing women in the context of marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The description 'believing' is considered a condition that negates the ruling for non-believers
C) The description 'believing' is not considered a condition; general permission applies
D) There is no difference of opinion on this

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits marrying a Kitabiyyah slave woman because the description 'believing' in the Quran does not function as a condition; the general permission prevails.

Marriage While in Ihram: Ruling and Proof
Explains that marriage is permissible for a muhrim, with hadith evidence and reconciliation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage for a muhrim and reconcile conflicting hadith evidence.

Core Idea
A muhrim may contract marriage; the Prophet himself did so with Maymunah.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position that a person in ihram can validly marry. It presents the primary evidence from the Prophet's marriage to Maymunah and explains how the conflicting hadith 'la yankih' is reconciled by interpreting 'nikah' as intercourse rather than the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the matn
Read the phrase 'al-muharramah wa law muhriman' and note that the commentator explains it means it is permissible to marry a woman even if the man is muhrim.
The ruling is permissibility (mubah).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet married Maymunah while in ihram. This is the main evidence.
The hadith is agreed upon by the six major collections.
3
Evaluate the strength of the dalil
Note that the hadith is strong: it is narrated by all six authentic collections, and al-Bukhari adds that the Prophet consummated after leaving ihram.
The conflicting narration by Yazid is weaker.
4
Identify the conflicting hadith
The hadith 'la yankih' (the muhrim does not marry nor give in marriage) seems to contradict the permissibility.
This hadith is recorded by most except al-Bukhari.
5
Apply the reconciliation
Understand that the mashayikh interpret 'nikah' in the hadith as intercourse, not the contract. Alternatively, if taken as contract, the prohibition indicates karahah (dislike) to reconcile evidences.
The Prophet's action is given precedence due to his superior memory and the principle that his actions indicate ibahah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمحرمة ولو محرما) أي حل تزوجها ولو كان الزوج محرماً لحديث الجماعة عن ابن عباس أنه عليه السلام تزوج ميمونة وهو محرم زاد البخاري وبنى بها وهو حلال وماتت بسرف» وأما ما رواه يزيد بن الأصم من أنه تزوجها وهو حلال فلم يَقْوَ قُوَّةَ هذا فإنه مما اتفق عليه الستة، وحديث يزيد لم يخرجه البخاري ولا النسائي، وأيضاً لا يقاوم بابن عباس حفظاً وإتقانا وقد أطال في فتح القدير في وجوه ترجحه، وذكروا ترجيحه في الأصول من باب البيان في تعارض النفي والإثبات وأما ما رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري أنه عليه السلام) قال المحرم لا ينكح ولا يُنْكِح فحمله المشايخ على الوطء في الجملة الأولى فالمنهي الرجل، وعلى التمكين منه في الجملة الثانية فالمنهي المرأة والتذكير باعتبار الشخص وكلمة (لا) فيه جاز أن تكون ناهية ودخولها على المسند للغائب جائز عند المحققين وإن كان غيره أكثر وجاز أن تكون نافية وفي النهاية والمعراج : إن المعنى الثانية لا يمكن المرأة من نفسه لتطأه كما هو فعل البعض فجعل التذكير على حقيقته وأن المنهي الرجل فيهما، والياء مفتوحة في الجملة الأولى مضمومة في الثانية مع كسر الكاف نفياً للإنكاح، ومن فتح الكاف من الثانية فقد صحف. وجوز في فتح القدير حمل النكاح فيه على العقد ويكون النهي فيه للكراهية جمعاً بين الدلائل، وذلك لأن المحرم في شغل عن مباشرة عقود الأنكحة لأنه يوجب شغل قلبه وهو محمل قوله ولا يخطب ولا يلزم كونه عليه السلام باشره لعدم شغل قلبه بخلافنا ا هـ . وحمل في غاية البيان قوله ولا يخطب على النهي عن التماس الوطء توفيقاً بين الأحاديث .
His statement: (And the prohibited woman even if [the man is] muhrim) i.e., it is permissible to marry her even if the husband is in the state of ihram, due to the hadith of the group from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet (peace be upon him) married Maymunah while he was muhrim—al-Bukhari added that he consummated with her while halal and she died in Sarif. As for what Yazid ibn al-Asamm narrated that he married her while halal, it did not attain the strength of this [hadith] because the six [authentic collections] agreed upon it, and the hadith of Yazid was not recorded by al-Bukhari nor al-Nasa'i, and also he cannot match Ibn 'Abbas in memory and precision. He [the author] elaborated in Fath al-Qadir on the aspects giving preponderance, and they mentioned its preponderance in the principles from the chapter of bayan in the conflict of negation and affirmation. As for what the group except al-Bukhari narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The muhrim does not marry nor give in marriage,' the mashayikh interpreted it as [referring to] intercourse in the first clause, so the prohibited one is the man; and on [allowing] enabling in the second clause, so the prohibited one is the woman, and the masculine gender is due to the person, and the word 'la' therein can be prohibitive and its entering upon the predicate for the absent is permissible according to the verifiers even if other is more frequent, and it can be negative. In al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj: that the meaning of the second clause is that the woman does not enable herself to be mounted by him as is the action of some, so they made the masculine upon its reality and that the prohibited one is the man in both. The ya' is fatha in the first clause and damma in the second with kasr of the kaf for negation of giving in marriage, and whoever puts fatha on the kaf of the second has made a mistake. And in Fath al-Qadir, it is permitted to interpret 'nikah' therein as the contract and the prohibition therein for karahah to reconcile the evidences, because the muhrim is occupied from directly conducting marriage contracts since it occupies his heart, and this is the meaning of his statement 'and he does not propose.' And it is not necessary that the Prophet (peace be upon him) did it because his heart was not occupied, unlike us. And in Ghayat al-Bayan, he interpreted his statement 'and he does not propose' as prohibition of seeking intercourse to reconcile between the hadiths.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining the Kanz al-Daqa'iq statement 'al-muharramah wa law muhriman'—i.e., it is permissible to marry a woman even if the man is in the state of ihram. The commentator provides evidence from the well-known hadith that the Prophet ﷺ married Maymunah while in ihram, which is agreed upon by the six major hadith collections, with al-Bukhari adding that he consummated after leaving ihram. The conflicting hadith narrated by Yazid is weaker. The other hadith 'the muhrim does not marry nor give in marriage' is reconciled by interpreting 'nikah' as intercourse in the first clause and enabling in the second. The mashayikh explain that the prohibition is on intercourse, not the contract. Some later scholars permitted interpreting it as the contract but with karahah (dislike) to gather between evidences, because the muhrim's engagement in contracts distracts from worship, but the Prophet ﷺ was not so occupied. This reconciliation maintains the permissibility of the marriage contract.

Key Terms
مُحْرِم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
نِكَاح (nikah): Marriage contract; also can mean intercourse (context-dependent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid (batil) because the muhrim cannot marry.
B) It is permissible (ja'iz) based on the Prophet's marriage to Maymunah while in ihram.
C) It is makruh tahrimi.
D) It is only allowed if the woman is also in ihram.

Key Takeaway
Marriage contract is permissible for a muhrim; the prohibition applies to intercourse.

Marriage to a Slave Woman: Hanafi Permissibility vs. Shafi'i Prohibition
Explains permissibility of slave marriage and usul dispute with Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits marriage to a slave woman (including Kitabiyya) and how the dispute with the Shafi'i school rests on the usul concept of mafhum al-shart wa al-wasf.

Core Idea
Hanafi marriage to a slave woman is permissible, grounded in general Quranic commands, not restricted by conditional descriptions.

This lesson examines a classical inter-madhhab dispute regarding the permissibility of marrying a slave woman, especially a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian). While the Shafi'i school considers it invalid based on the condition of 'believing' in Quran 4:25, the Hanafi school holds it permissible but slightly disliked when a free woman is available. The difference stems from a foundational usul al-fiqh disagreement: does the presence of a condition in a verse generate a legally binding implication (mafhum) that negates the ruling when the condition is absent? Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi jurists apply general texts and treat conditions as descriptive rather than restrictive.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on marrying a slave woman according to the Hanafi school: it is permissible (mubah) but makruh tanzihi when a free woman is available.
The permissibility includes Kitabiyya slave women.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State the Shafi'i position: marriage to a non-believing slave woman is invalid (batil), based on the condition 'believing' in Quran 4:25.
3
Explore the usul foundation
Explain the disagreement over mafhum al-shart wa al-wasf: Shafi'i accepts the concept as proof (ruling negated when condition absent), Hanafi rejects it.
This is the root of the ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Show how Hanafis rely on general texts like Quran 4:24 and argue that negation of permissibility does not entail prohibition, only karahah.
The general command 'marry what is good for you' is primary.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: Hanafi allows marriage to Kitabiyya slave woman (with karahah), Shafi'i prohibits it.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi is permissibility with karahah tanzihiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والأمة ولو كتابية أي حل تزوجها خلافاً للشافعي، وأصله التقييد بالوصف والشرط في قوله تعالى ومن لم يستطع منكم طولا أن ينكح المحصنات المؤمنات فما ملكت أيمانكم من فتياتكم المؤمنات ) [النساء : ٢٥] والخلاف مبني على مسألة أصولية هي أن مفهوم الشرط والوصف هل يكون معتبراً ينتفي الحكم بانتفائه ؟ فقال الشافعي : نعم. وقلنا : لا فصار الحل ثابتاً فيها بالعمومات مثل قوله فانكحوا ما طاب لكم من النساء﴾ [النساء: ٢٤] فلذلك جوزنا نكاح الأمة مع طول الحرة ونكاح الأمة الكتابية وتمامه في الأصول. وعلى تقدير اعتبار مفهومهما فمقتضاهما عدم الإباحة الثابتة عند وجود القيد المبيح وعدم الإباحة أعم . من ثبوت الحرمة أو الكراهة ولا دلالة للأعم على الأخص بخصوصه فيجوز ثبوت الكراهة عند عدم الضرورة وعند وجود طول الحرة كما يجوز ثبوت الحرمة على السواء والكراهة أقل فتعينت فقلنا بها. وبالكراهة صرح في البدائع، كذا في فتح القدير وقد يقال مقتضاهما عدم الحل لا عدم الإباحة وعدم الحل مدعاه، والظاهر أن الكراهة في كلام البدائع تنزيهية فلم يخرج عن المباح بالكلية وإن كان الترك راجحاً على الفعل نعم عدم الإباحة أعم من الحرام والمكروه تحريماً، والظاهر من كلام الفقهاء أن المباح عندهم ما أذن الشارع في فعله لا ما استوى فعله وتركه كما هو في الأصول، والخلف لفظي كما عرف في بحث الأمر من البدائع وغيره
His statement: (And the slave woman even if Kitabiyya) i.e., it is permissible to marry her, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The origin is restriction by description and condition in Allah's statement: 'And whoever among you cannot afford to marry free believing women, then from what your right hands possess of believing slave girls' [4:25]. The dispute is based on an usuli issue: Is the concept of condition and description considered so that the ruling is negated by its negation? Al-Shafi'i said yes, and we said no, so permissibility is established for her by general texts like His statement: 'Marry what is good for you of women' [4:24]. Therefore we allowed marriage to a slave woman even when a free woman is available, and marriage to a Kitabiyya slave woman. The completion is in usul. On the assumption of considering their concept, their implication is the negation of permissibility established when the permitting condition exists, and negation of permissibility is more general than the establishment of prohibition or karahah, and the general does not indicate the specific in its particularity. So it is permissible to establish karahah when there is no necessity and when there is availability of a free woman, just as it is permissible to establish prohibition equally, and karahah is lesser so it was determined and we said it. Karahah is explicitly stated in al-Bada'i, likewise in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said that their implication is negation of halal, not negation of ibahah, and negation of halal is what is claimed. And the apparent [meaning] is that karahah in the speech of al-Bada'i is tanzihiyyah, so it does not remove it from mubah entirely, although leaving is preferable to doing. Indeed, negation of ibahah is more general than haram and makruh tahrimi, and the apparent from the speech of the fuqaha is that mubah according to them is what the Lawgiver permitted to do, not what is equal in doing and leaving as in usul, and the dispute is verbal as known in the discussion of command from al-Bada'i and others.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute addressing the permissibility of marrying a slave woman (including a Kitabiyya). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that it is allowed, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The commentator explains that the dispute originates from a usul al-fiqh issue: whether the concept of a condition or description (e.g., 'believing' in the ayah) is legally operative such that the ruling is negated when the condition is absent. The Shafi'i school holds that it is (they take the concept as proof), so the ayah would only permit slave women who are believers and only when one cannot afford a free believing woman. The Hanafi school holds that conditions and descriptions do not generate a concept (mafhum mukhalafah) in the sense of negation; thus, the general texts (like 'marry what is good for you') apply, allowing marriage to any slave woman, even non-believing, and even when a free woman is available. However, the Hanafis still consider it makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) to marry a slave woman when a free woman is available, based on the wording of the ayah, but this is a verbal difference with Shafi'i. The passage clarifies that the Hanafi position is permissibility with dislike, not prohibition.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave.
مفهوم الشرط والوصف (mafhum al-shart wa al-wasf): The inferred meaning from a condition or description—whether the ruling is negated when the condition/description is absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the root usul issue in the dispute over marrying a slave woman?
A) Whether the Quranic verse is abrogated.
B) Whether mafhum al-shart wa al-wasf is considered a valid proof.
C) Whether a slave woman can be married without a wali.
D) Whether the mahr is valid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi permissibility of marrying a slave woman is grounded in general Quranic texts, and the dispute with Shafi'i originates from the different treatment of condition concepts in usul al-fiqh.

Marrying a Free Woman Over a Slave Woman: Ruling and Evidence
Explains that it is permissible to marry a free woman over a slave woman, but not vice versa, with evidence and details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on co-wives when one is free and one is a slave, and explain the evidence from hadith and the principle of tansif al-ni'mah.

Core Idea
A free wife takes precedence over a slave wife – the hadith forbids placing a slave woman over a free woman.

This lesson addresses a specific aspect of polygyny: when a man has a free wife and wishes to add a slave wife, or vice versa. The Hanafi school, relying on an explicit hadith and the principle that slavery halves the blessing (tansif al-ni'mah), permits marrying a free woman over a slave woman but absolutely forbids the reverse. We will examine the primary evidence, the reasoning behind it, and how the Hanafi position differs from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what action is being ruled upon (marrying a co-wife) and whether it is permissible or prohibited. There are two scenarios: marrying a free woman over a slave woman (permissible) and marrying a slave woman over a free woman (prohibited).
The ruling applies when the free marriage is valid (nikah sahih).
2
Examine the Hadith Evidence
The source cites the hadith: 'The slave woman is not married over the free woman, and the free woman is married over the slave woman.' Understand that this is the primary textual proof (dalil).
The hadith is ahad but explicit; the Hanafi school considers its meaning clear and binding.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The principle of tansif al-ni'mah – slavery halves the blessing in marriage (e.g., reduced rights and responsibilities). This explains why a slave woman cannot be elevated over a free woman, even in the co-wife hierarchy.
This principle is also used in divorce rulings.
4
Consider Intra-Madhhab Disputes
The text notes that Imam al-Shafi'i allowed a slave man to marry a free woman over a slave woman, and Imam Malik allowed it if the free woman consents. Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith is general and not conditioned on consent or the husband's status.
The Hanafi view is that slavery is a deficiency that prevents the slave woman from being placed above a free woman regardless of circumstances.
5
Apply the Ruling to Scenarios
Work through the sub-cases in the text: (a) marrying a slave woman without permission then later marrying a free woman, (b) marrying the free daughter of a slave woman before permission, (c) marrying four slaves and five free women in one contract. Use the rule to determine validity.
Each scenario tests the application of the 'no slave over free' principle and the effect of suspension (waqf) in marriage contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: (And the free woman over the slave woman, not the reverse) i.e., it is permissible to admit a free woman [as co-wife] over a slave woman, and it is not permissible to admit a slave woman over a free woman who is married with a valid marriage, due to the hadith: 'The slave woman is not married over the free woman, and the free woman is married over the slave woman.' This passage contains the ruling that a free woman can be taken as a co-wife alongside a slave woman, but a slave woman cannot be taken as a co-wife over a free woman. The commentator also mentions Malik who allowed it if the free woman consents. The Hanafi position is that slavery reduces half the blessing (i.e., in marriage, due to the reduced ability to provide), so a slave woman's lower status prevents her from being placed over a free woman. The passage also discusses cases: if a man marries a slave woman without permission and then marries a free woman, subsequent permission does not validate the first marriage because it becomes a marriage of a slave over a free woman. However, if he marries the daughter (of the slave woman who is free) before permission, it is valid because the first marriage is suspended. Also, if one marries four slave women and five free women in one contract, only the marriage to the slave women is valid because the five free women exceed the limit. Key Terms
تنصيف النعمة (tansif al-ni'mah): Halving of the blessing; slavery reduces the marital rights and responsibilities, so a slave woman's status is lower.
الأمة (al-ammah): A female slave.
الحرة (al-hurrah): A free woman.

Marrying a Slave Woman During the Free Wife's 'Idda
Expands the matn's ruling on marrying a slave woman during 'idda.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a slave woman during the free wife's 'idda and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding irrevocable divorce.

Core Idea
Marrying a slave woman during a free wife's 'idda is absolutely forbidden, regardless of the type of divorce.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the prohibition of marrying a slave woman while one's free wife is in her waiting period. The passage shows that the Hanafi school applies this rule regardless of the type of divorce, though there is a dispute among the early authorities regarding irrevocable divorce. Understanding this ruling clarifies the school's view on the ongoing legal connection during 'idda.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: 'wa law fi 'iddat al-hurrah' – even during the free woman's 'idda, it is not permissible to marry a slave woman.
2
Note the absolute scope
The commentator says 'atlaqah so afada anna la farq' – the ruling is absolute, covering both revocable and irrevocable divorce.
3
Distinguish the two 'idda types
In revocable divorce (raj'i), there is consensus that it is prohibited because the wife remains a wife. In irrevocable divorce (ba'in), there is an intra-Hanafi dispute.
4
Understand the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is not prohibited in ba'in divorce, because it is not 'marrying over her' – the marriage has ended.
5
Reconcile with the relied-upon Hanafi position
Despite the Sahibayn's view, the matn (and thus the relied-upon position) maintains absolute prohibition, as the 'idda retains a legal bond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو) في عدة الحرة) أي لا يحل إدخال الأمة في عدة الحرة. أطلقه فأفاد أنه لا فرق أن تكون العدة عن طلاق رجعي أو بائن، ولا خلاف في المنع في الأول لأن المطلقة رجعياً زوجة، وفي الثاني خلاف قالا : لا يحرم لأن هذا ليس بتزوج عليها وهو المحرم ولهذا لو حلف أن لا يتزوج عليها لم يحنث بهذا بخلاف تزوج الأخت في عدة الأخت من طلاق بائن.
His statement: (And even if) in the 'idda of a free woman) i.e., it is not permissible to admit a slave woman [as wife] during the 'idda of a free woman. He made it absolute, so it indicates that there is no difference whether the 'idda is from a revocable or irrevocable divorce. There is no dispute regarding the prohibition in the first because a revocably divorced woman is a wife. In the second, there is a dispute: the two (Sahibayn) said: it is not prohibited because this is not a marriage over her—and that is what is prohibited—and therefore if he swore not to marry over her, he would not break his oath with this, unlike marrying the sister during the 'idda of the sister from an irrevocable divorce.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This short passage elaborates on the ruling that one cannot marry a slave woman during the 'idda (waiting period) of his free wife. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states 'wa law fi 'iddat al-hurrah' (even if during the free woman's 'idda). The commentator explains that this applies equally whether the 'idda is from a revocable divorce (raj'i) or an irrevocable divorce (ba'in). In the revocable case, all agree it is prohibited because the wife is still considered a wife. In the irrevocable case, there is a dispute: the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, i.e., the Sahibayn) hold that it is not prohibited because this is not a marriage 'over her' in the sense of taking a co-wife over an existing wife, since the marriage has ended. However, the relied-upon Hanafi position (as indicated by the matn) is that it is still prohibited, because the 'idda maintains some connection. The commentator notes that unlike marrying the sister during the 'idda of another sister, which is also prohibited in irrevocable divorce? The text seems cut off. The key point is the absolute prohibition of marrying a slave woman during the free woman's 'idda in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband.
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): Revocable divorce where the husband can take his wife back without a new contract during the 'idda.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): Irrevocable divorce where the husband does not have the right to take back his wife; a new marriage is needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Prohibited, because the 'idda creates a continuing bond.
C) Permissible only if the free woman consents.
D) Makruh but not haram.

Key Takeaway
Marrying a slave woman during a free wife's 'idda is prohibited in the Hanafi school, regardless of whether the 'idda is from a revocable or irrevocable divorce, due to the continuing legal bond of 'idda.

Marrying Free and Slave Women: The Hanafi Position and Inter-Madhhab Dispute
Commentary on the permission and prohibition of marrying free and slave women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a slave woman over a free woman, identify the contrary positions of al-Shafi'i and Malik, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the hadith and the principle that slavery halves the blessing.

Core Idea
Marrying a slave woman over a free woman is disliked, not prohibited, because the hadith's prohibition is general and interpreted as karahah tanzihiyyah.

In this passage, the commentator explains the Hanafi position on the delicate issue of a free man marrying a slave woman when he already has a free wife. The core ruling is that while it is permissible to bring a free woman into a marriage with a slave wife, the reverse is not lawful and is deemed makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked). This ruling is derived from a prophetic hadith and is contrasted with the views of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for appreciating how Hanafi usul al-fiqh interprets general prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two scenarios: marrying a free woman over a slave woman (halal) and marrying a slave woman over a free woman (makruh tanzihi). Note that the latter is disliked, not haram.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i allowed a slave man to marry a slave woman over a free woman, and Malik allowed it with the free woman's consent.
Both opponents differ from the Hanafi absolute prohibition for a free man.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The text does not give their proofs explicitly, but al-Shafi'i likely argued that the slave man is not fully qualified to marry a free woman, while Malik required consent to avoid injustice.
The Hanafi response addresses these points.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi argument uses the hadith as an absolute proof that applies to free and slave men alike. Additionally, slavery reduces the blessing of marriage by half, making it inappropriate to marry a slave over a free woman.
The hadith is cited as: لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة وتنكح الحرة على الأمة
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, a free man who marries a slave woman over a free woman commits a disliked act (but the marriage is valid). Under Shafi'i, a slave man may do so without dislike; under Maliki, it is permissible only with the free wife's consent.
The tarjih is the Hanafi position as mufta bihi.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute explaining the Hanafi ruling on marrying a free woman over a slave woman and vice versa. The commentator begins by citing the Quranic verse 'And lawful for you are those beyond that' to justify the permissibility of marrying a slave woman when one has a free wife, as well as marrying a Kitabi slave woman. He then discusses the concept of 'karahah' (dislike) when a free man marries a slave woman despite having a free wife. The Hanafi position, as stated in al-Bada'i', is that this is makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked) and not haram, because the evidence is general and can be interpreted as dislike rather than prohibition. The commentator then addresses the matn phrase 'وَالْحُرَّةُ عَلَى الْأَمَةِ لَا عَكْسَهُ' meaning it is permissible to marry a free woman over a slave woman but not the reverse. This is supported by the hadith 'لا تنكح الأمة على الحرة وتنكح الحرة على الأمة'. This hadith is used as evidence against Imam al-Shafi'i, who allowed a slave man to marry a slave woman over a free woman, and against Imam Malik, who allowed it with the free woman's consent. The Hanafi reasoning is that slavery reduces the blessing (of marriage) by half, so marrying a slave woman over a free woman diminishes the free wife's status. The passage then discusses various scenarios: if a man marries a slave woman without permission and then marries a free woman, the slave marriage is invalidated; if he marries the daughter of his slave wife while she is free, it is valid because the suspended marriage is non-existent. Finally, it addresses the case of marrying four slave women and five free women in one contract: the marriage of the slave women is valid because the five free marriages are invalid. The passage concludes with a note on variant readings of the matn.

Key Terms
كراهة تنزيهية (karahah tanzihiyyah): Mild dislike, where the act is discouraged but not sinful, and leaving it is preferable.
حل (hill): Lawfulness or permissibility of an action, synonymous with ibahah in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a free man marrying a slave woman when he already has a free wife?
A) It is haram and invalid.
B) It is makruh tanzihi (mildly disliked).
C) It is permitted without any dislike.
D) It is only allowed if the free wife consents.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that a free man marrying a slave woman over a free woman is valid but makruh tanzihi, based on the hadith and the principle that slavery halves the blessing of marriage.

Limit of Four Wives and Permissibility of Concubines
Explanation of the Quranic limit of four wives and the permissibility of slave concubines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic basis for limiting marriage to four wives and distinguish the permissibility of unlimited concubines according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Four wives maximum, unlimited concubines: justice required for wives, not for concubines.

This lesson examines the classical Hanafi ruling on the maximum number of wives a man may marry, as well as the permissibility of taking slave women as concubines. The source passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the proof from Quran 4:3, linguistic analysis of the verse, and the distinction between wives and concubines. You will learn how the Hanafi school derives the limit of four and allows unlimited concubines, and why this distinction is based on the textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its category
State the ruling: a man may marry up to four women (free or slave), and may take any number of slave concubines. The ruling is a permission (mubah) with a condition of justice.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary evidence is Quran 4:3. Recite the verse and note the key phrases: 'فَانكِحُوا مَا طَابَ لَكُم مِّنَ النِّسَاءِ مَثْنَىٰ وَثُلَاثَ وَرُبَاعَ' and the exception 'فَوَاحِدَةً'.
The imperative 'انكحوا' indicates permissibility, not obligation.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The words 'مثنى وثلاث ورباع' are circumstantial qualifiers indicating a distributive sense: a man may marry two, three, or four, either in combination or separately. This restricts the number to four, as recognized by consensus.
The phrase 'فإن خفتم ألا تعدلوا فواحدة' makes justice a condition, and if feared, then only one wife.
4
Distinguish between wives and concubines
The same verse continues with 'أَوْ مَا مَلَكَتْ أَيْمَانُكُمْ' which is interpreted as absolute permission to take as many concubines as desired, without limit. Justice (qism) is required only for wives, not concubines.
The fatwa cited confirms that a man with four wives and many concubines may buy another concubine without blame.
5
Understand the consensus and the opposition
The four imams and the majority of Muslims agree on this ruling. The Rawafid (Shia) oppose it, but the Hanafi school dismisses their view as baseless.
The lesson does not detail the Shia position, only notes it is not followed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأربع من الحرائر والإماء) أي وحل تزوج أربع لا أكثر لقوله تعالى فانكحوا ما طاب لكم . النساء مثنى وثلاث ورباع) [النساء : ٣] اتفق عليه الأئمة الأربعة وجمهور المسلمين، ولا اعتبار بخلاف الروافض، ولا حاجة إلى الإطالة في الرد عليهم. قال القاضي البيضاوي: مثنى وثلاث ورباع معدولة عن أعداد مكررة هي ثنتين ثنتين، وثلاث ثلاث، وأربع أربع، وهي غير منصرفة للعدل والصفة فإنها بنيت صفات وإن كانت أصولها لم تبين لها . وقيل : لتكرار العدل فإنها معدولة باعتبار الصيغة والتكرير منصوبة على الحال من فاعل طاب، ومعناها الإذن لكل ناكح يريد الجمع أن ينكح ما شاء من العدد المذكورين متفقين ومختلفين كقوله : اقتسموا هذه البدرة در همين در همين وثلاثة ثلاثة ولو أفرد كان المعنى تجويز الجمع بين هذه الأعداد دون التوزيع، ولو ذكرت بـ أو الذهب تجويز الاختلاف في العدد ا هـ. وفي فتح القدير: وحاصل الحال أن حل الواحدة كان معلوماً وهذه الآية لبيان حل الزائد عليها إلى حد معين مع بيان التخيير بين الجمع والتفريق في ذلك. وإنما كان العدد الآية مانعاً من الزيادة وإن كان من حيث هو عدد لا يمنعها لوقوعه حالاً قيداً في الإحلال. قيد بالتزوج لأن له التسري بما شاء من الإماء لإطلاق قوله تعالى أو ما ملكت أيمانكم [النساء : ٣] وفي الفتاوى : رجل له أربع نسوة وألف جارية وأراد أن يشتري جارية أخرى فلامه رجل يخاف عليه الكفر ا هـ . ولم أر حكم ما إذا أراد أن يتزوج على امرأته الأخرى فلامه رجل وينبغي أن لا يخاف عليه الكفر لما أن في تزوج الجمع من النساء مشقة شديدة بسبب وجوب العدل بينهن، ولذا قال تعالى فإن خفتم أن لا تعدلوا فواحدة قوله : (وينبغي أن لا يخاف عليه الكفر (الخ قال في النهر : الدليل المقتضي للحوق الإماء مع الزوجات واحد فأنى وقع الفرق بينهما؟ وما فرق به من ! أن في الجمع بن الحرائر مشقه سبب وجوب العدل بينهما بخلاف الجمع بين السراري فإنه لا قسم بينهن مما لا أثر له النص .
His statement: (And four from free women and slave women) meaning it is lawful to marry four, no more, due to His saying: 'Marry what seems good to you of women, two, three, or four' [Quran 4:3]. The four imams and the majority of Muslims agree on this, and no attention is paid to the opposition of the Rawafid. There is no need to lengthen refuting them. Al-Qadi al-Baydawi said: 'Mathna, thulatha, ruba'a' are derived from repeated numbers: two two, three three, four four. They are indeclinable due to being both changed in form and adjectives, although their origins are not declined. It is said: due to the repetition of the change, they are changed in form and repetition, and are in the accusative case as a circumstantial qualifier (hal) of the subject of 'taba'. Their meaning is permission for every marrying man who wants to combine to marry whatever of the mentioned numbers, agreeing or differing, like his statement: 'Divide this full purse into twos and threes.' If it were said singly, the meaning would be permission of combination between these numbers without distribution. If mentioned with 'or', it would imply permission of variation in number. End quote. And in Fath al-Qadir: The summary is that the permissibility of one was known, and this verse is to explain the permissibility of more than one up to a specified limit, with a choice between combining and separating in that. And the number in the verse prevents increase, even though as a number it does not prevent, because it occurs as a circumstantial qualifier restricting the permission. He restricted by marriage because he may take concubines as he wishes from slave women, due to the absolute nature of His saying: 'or what your right hands possess' [Quran 4:3]. And in al-Fatawa: A man has four wives and a thousand slave girls, and he wants to buy another slave girl. A man rebuked him, fearing kufr for him. End quote. I have not seen the ruling if he wants to marry another woman over his wife, and a man rebuked him. It should be that one should not fear kufr for him, because in marrying multiple women there is severe difficulty due to the obligation of justice between them, hence He said: 'If you fear you will not be just, then one.' His statement: (It should be that one should not fear kufr for him etc.) Al-Nahr said: The evidence that necessitates including slave women with wives is one, so how can there be a difference between them? And what distinction is made that in combining free women there is difficulty due to the obligation of justice between them, unlike combining slave women because there is no division between them, which has no effect from the text.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the maximum number of wives a man may have. The matn phrase 'وأربع من الحرائر والإماء' means it is permissible to marry up to four free women or slave women (or a combination). The primary evidence is Quran 4:3: 'Marry what seems good to you of women, two, three, or four.' This is agreed upon by the four Sunni schools and the majority of Muslims; the opposing view of the Rafidah (Shi'a) is dismissed. The commentator then provides a linguistic analysis by al-Qadi al-Baydawi of the words 'مثنى وثلاث ورباع' (two, three, four), explaining that they are accusative circumstantial qualifiers indicating that a man may marry two, three, or four, either all at once or in combination. The verse allows up to four, but if one fears injustice, he should marry only one. The commentator then distinguishes between marriage (nikah) and concubinage (tasrī): while the limit applies to marriage, a man may have as many slave concubines as he wishes, based on the same verse's conclusion 'or what your right hands possess'. A fatwa is cited: a man with four wives and a thousand slave girls who wishes to buy another slave girl should not be rebuked with fear of kufr, because the difficulty of justice applies only to wives. The passage ends with a discussion of the distinction: combining free women requires justice in division of time and expenses, which is burdensome, whereas combining slave women does not require such division.

Key Terms
مثنى وثلاث ورباع (mathnā wa thulāthā wa rubā'): Words meaning 'two, three, four' in a distributive sense, indicating that a man may marry two, three, or four women, either in combination or separately.
تسري (tasrī): Taking slave women as concubines, which is permissible without limit based on Quran 4:3 'or what your right hands possess'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He may marry up to four wives and up to four concubines.
C) He may marry up to three wives.
D) He may marry up to nine wives as per some narrations.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a man to marry up to four wives (with justice required) and allows him unlimited concubines, based on Quran 4:3.

Maximum Number of Wives for Free Men and Slaves
Commentary on permissibility limits for marriage and concubinage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum number of wives permissible for a free man and a slave in the Hanafi school, identify the Quranic proof, and explain the distinction between wives and concubines.

Core Idea
A free man may marry up to four; a slave may marry up to two—Quran and consensus set the limits.

The number of wives a man may marry at once is not left to personal discretion; it is explicitly limited by divine text and scholarly consensus. In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on this limit for both free men and slaves, the proof from Surah al-Nisa, and the crucial distinction between marriage and concubinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act under discussion (marrying women) and the hukm (ruling) for each category: free man may marry up to four; slave may marry up to two. Note that exceeding these limits is prohibited (haram).
The ruling applies to both free and slave women for the free man, but a slave may marry either free or slave women, up to two.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse 4:3 is the direct proof. Recite it and note that the four Imams and the majority of Muslims agree on its interpretation. The opposition of the Rafidah is dismissed.
The verse uses the imperative 'fankihu', which in Hanafi usul indicates ibaha (permissibility) not obligation.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how the words 'mathna, thulath, ruba' (two, three, four) function as a limit. They are in the accusative case as a state (hal), restricting the permission to these numbers. The verse permits either combining or separating the numbers.
The grammatical analysis by al-Baydawi clarifies that the numbers are not distributively permissive but cumulative up to four.
4
Distinguish wives from concubines
The verse uses 'ma malakat aymanukum' separately, indicating that a free man may have unlimited concubines (slave women he owns) without marriage. This is not a violation of the four-wife limit.
The commentary notes that buying a slave girl when already with four wives does not incur blame, but marrying a fifth woman would be problematic due to hardship of justice.
5
Apply the ruling to slaves
For a male slave (including mudabbar and mukatab), the maximum is two wives, based on consensus of the Companions and the principle that slavery reduces legal capacity. A slave also cannot own concubines.
The restriction applies only to marriage; the slave's master may not give him a concubine either.

The Book of Nikah / Section on Prohibited Women. Regarding the iddah of a free woman, and four from free women, and if not then only water for the free man, and two for the slave, and a pregnant woman from zina not from... ba'in then it is not permissible by consensus. From the Book of Nikah, Section on Prohibited Women. [4:3] Unlike combining slave girls, for there is no division between them, even though they said: If he refrains from marrying over his wife so as not to cause grief to his existing wife, he is rewarded, and it is not appropriate to blame anyone for that, due to His statement: 'And those who guard their private parts except from their spouses or what their right hands possess, then they are not blameworthy' (23:5-6). His saying: (And two for the slave) meaning a man marries two, whether they are free or slave women, and it is not permissible for him to marry more than that in marriage due to the consensus of the Companions, and because slavery diminishes favor and punishment. He said absolutely regarding the slave, so it includes the mudabbar and mukatab, and he restricted it to marriage because it is not permissible for him to have concubines, nor for his master to give him a concubine, and the mukatab and slave do not own anything except divorce, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. The summary is that the permissibility is confined to marriage contract and right hand possession, and the second is not for the slave because he does not own, and if he owns, the permissibility is only in marriage contract.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) discussing the number of wives permissible for free men and slaves. The author begins by clarifying that a free man may marry up to four wives, based on Quran 4:3, which explicitly allows up to four. The consensus of the four Imams and the majority of Muslims is cited, with a dismissal of the Rafidah opposition. The grammatical analysis of the verse is provided from al-Baydawi: the words 'mathna, thulath, ruba' are derived from repeated numbers and are indeclinable, indicating permission to marry two, three, or four as one wishes, whether in combination or separately. The commentary then addresses a hypothetical: if a man already has four wives and many slave girls, buying another slave girl does not lead to disbelief, as the verse allows unlimited concubines. A distinction is made between marrying over a wife (which may cause hardship due to justice obligations) and acquiring a slave girl (where no such division is required). However, the author notes that refraining from causing grief to a wife is meritorious. For slaves, the maximum is two wives, whether free or slave, based on the consensus of the Companions and the principle that slavery reduces capacity. The slave is defined broadly to include the mudabbar (one whose emancipation is delayed) and the mukatab (one who has a contract of manumission). The slave may not take concubines, as he does not own anything except the right to divorce. Thus, his permissible conjugal relations are confined to marriage.

Key Terms
حرائر (hara'ir): Free women, as opposed to slave women.
إماء (ima'): Slave women (female slaves).
عبد (abd): A male slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many wives may a free Hanafi man marry at one time?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) As many as he can support

Key Takeaway
A free man may marry up to four wives; a slave may marry up to two, with unlimited concubines for the free, but slaves have no concubine rights.
