Marrying a Woman Pregnant from Zina: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on marrying a pregnant woman from zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on marrying a woman pregnant from zina and identify the Mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Marrying a woman pregnant from zina is permissible; the fornicator's 'crop' has no protected rights.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a subtle but practically important issue: can a man marry a woman who is pregnant from an unlawful relationship (zina)? While one might assume any pregnancy bars marriage, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on the cause. The majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) permits it, while Abu Yusuf analogizes to other pregnancies and declares it invalid. Understanding their reasoning deepens your grasp of how the school harmonizes textual evidence (hadith) with qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Note the two conflicting positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it is permitted (mubah), and (2) Abu Yusuf says it is invalid (fasid).
The ikhtilaf applies only when the husband is not the fornicator. The fornicator himself can marry her by agreement.
2
Grasp the majority proof (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
They rely on the hadith 'do not water another's crop' – the prohibition protects the legal father's lineage. Since zina establishes no lineage, the fornicator has no protected 'crop', so the ban does not apply.
Also, the pregnancy itself does not create a legal bar (iddah) because zina does not create a marriage bond.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's qiyas
Abu Yusuf draws an analogy (qiyas) to pregnancy from other causes (e.g., valid marriage, dubious intercourse). In those cases, the pregnancy is 'protected' (mahrum) and marriage is prohibited by consensus. He argues the same should apply to zina because the pregnancy is still factual.
The majority rejects this qiyas because the underlying cause (protected lineage) is absent.
4
Apply the hadith to distinguish
The hadith explicitly warns against watering another's crop – i.e., having intercourse with a pregnant woman whose child belongs to another. Since the zani has no recognized paternity, his 'crop' does not exist, so the warning does not extend to him.
This reasoning is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) over strict qiyas.
5
Identify the relied-upon position
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) presents the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad as the preferred one (mufta bihi). Abu Yusuf's view is considered weak (marjuh).
Thus, in fatwa, a man (who is not the fornicator) may validly marry a woman pregnant from zina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وحبلى من زنا لا من غيره أي وحل تزويج الحبلى من الزنا ولا يجوز تزوج الحبلى من غير الزنا. أما الأول فهو قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف : هو فاسد قياساً على الثاني وهي الحبلى من غيره، فإن تزوجها لا يصح إجماعاً لحرمة الحمل، وهذا الحمل محترم لأنه لا حناية منه ولهذا لم يجر إسقاطه. ولهما أنهما من المحللات بالنص وحرمة الوطء كيلا لا يسقي ماءه زرع غيره والامتناع في ثابت النسب لحق صاحب الماء ولا حرمة للزاني. ومحل الخلاف تزوج غير الزاني، أما تزوج الزاني لها فجائز اتفاقاً وتستحق النفقة عند الكل ويحل وطؤها عند الكل كما في النهاية . وقيد بالتزوج لأن وطأها حرام اتفاقاً للحديث من كان يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر فلا يستقين ماءه زرع غيره (۱) فإن قيل : فم الرحم ينسد بالحبل فكيف يكون سقى زرع غيره؟ قلنا : شعره ينبت من ماء الغير كذا في المعراج وحكم الدواعي على قولهما كالوطء كما في النهاية. وذكر التمرتاشي أنها لا نفقة لها، وقيل لها ذلك والأول أوجه لأن المانع من الوطء من جهتها بخلاف الحيض فإنه سماوي. كذا في فتح القدير. وأطلق في من غيره فشمل الحامل من حربي كالمهاجرة والمسبية وروي عن أبي حنيفة صحة العقد كالحامل من الزنا، وصحح الشارح المنع وهو المعتمد. وفي فتح القدير: إنه ظاهر المذهب. وشمل أم الولد فلو زوج أم ولده وهي حامل منه فالنكاح باطل لأنها فراش لمولاها حيث يثبت نسب ولدها منه من غير دعوى فلو صح النكاح لحصل الجمع بين الفراشين إلا أنه غير متأكد حتى ينتفي الولد بالنفي من غير لعان فلا يعتبر ما لم يتصل به الحمل. كذا قوله : (بدليل الأمة الخ) قال المقدسي فيما نقل عنه أقول : الفرق بينهما أن الحمل يخفى أمره
His saying: (And a pregnant woman from zina, not from other than it) meaning it is permissible to marry a pregnant woman from zina, and it is not permissible to marry a pregnant woman from other than zina. As for the first, it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually 'qawluhuma' refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad? Wait, in context: 'Ama al-awwal fahuwa qawluhuma' means the first (permissibility) is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually the text says 'qawluhuma' referring to the two disciples, but earlier it says 'he' for Abu Hanifa. Let me check: 'Ama al-awwal fahuwa qawluhuma. Wa qala Abu Yusuf: huwa fasid qiyasan ala al-thani' So the first is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? That is contradictory because it says Abu Yusuf said it is fasid. Actually, 'qawluhuma' likely refers to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, because Abu Yusuf disagrees. So: As for the first view (permissibility), it is the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). And Abu Yusuf said: It is invalid (fasid) by analogy to the second case. Then the commentary explains the reasoning. And the place of difference is marrying other than the fornicator; as for the fornicator himself marrying her, it is permissible by agreement, and she is entitled to maintenance, and intercourse is allowed according to all, as in al-Nihayah. And he restricted it to marriage because intercourse is prohibited by agreement due to the hadith: 'Whoever believes in Allah and the Last Day should not water another's crop.' If it is said: the mouth of the womb is closed by pregnancy, how is it watering another's crop? We say: his hair grows from the water of another, as in al-Mi'raj. And the ruling of the appetites according to the two is like intercourse, as in al-Nihayah. And al-Tamartashi mentioned that she has no maintenance, and it was said she has it, and the first is more appropriate because the preventer of intercourse is from her side, unlike menstruation, which is heavenly. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And he said absolutely in 'from other than it' so it includes the pregnant woman from a harbi (enemy), like the muhajira and sabiyya, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa the validity of the contract like the pregnant from zina, and the commentator affirmed the prohibition, which is relied upon. In Fath al-Qadir: it is the apparent madhhab. And it includes the umm walad; if he marries his umm walad while she is pregnant from him, the marriage is invalid because she is the bed (firash) of her master, as her child's lineage is established from him without need for claim, so if the marriage were valid, it would result in combining two beds, except that it is not established until the child is repudiated by denial without li'an, so it is not considered unless accompanied by pregnancy. Thus. His saying: (Based on the evidence of the slave girl etc.) Al-Maqdisi said in what is transmitted from him: I say: the difference between them is that the matter of pregnancy is hidden...
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on marrying a pregnant woman, distinguishing between pregnancy from zina (unlawful intercourse) and from other causes (valid marriage, dubious intercourse, etc.). The author explains that, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the 'two'), it is permissible to marry a woman pregnant from zina, but Abu Yusuf considers such a marriage invalid (fasid) by analogy to the case of pregnancy from other causes, which is unanimously prohibited. The key principle is that the pregnancy from zina is not 'protected' (non-mahrum) and does not establish lineage, so there is no concern about mixing lineages. The fornicator himself is allowed to marry her without dispute. However, for the man who committed zina, he can marry her, but if another man does so, the ikhtilaf occurs. The hadith prohibits 'watering another's crop,' but for the zani, there is no such respect. The commentary also discusses maintenance and intercourse. The passage further clarifies that this ruling applies to pregnancies from other causes, including from a harbi woman or even the umm walad (who is already a concubine). For the umm walad, the marriage is invalid because she is already a valid bed (firash) for her master, and combining two beds is not allowed. The text ends with a note about the distinction between the two types of pregnancy.

Key Terms
حبلى من زنا (hubla min zina): A pregnant woman whose pregnancy results from unlawful sexual intercourse (zina).
فراش (firash): Bed; a legal term meaning a woman whom a man has a valid marital or concubinage relationship, establishing lineage of her children.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has given birth to her master's child, thus attaining special status (she becomes free upon his death).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on a man marrying a woman pregnant from zina?
A) It is haram and invalid
B) It is permissible (mubah)
C) It is makruh but valid
D) It is valid only if the man is the fornicator

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits marrying a woman pregnant from zina because the pregnancy does not protect the fornicator's lineage, and the hadith's prohibition is inapplicable.

Nikah — Marriage

Slave may marry two wives
Ruling on slave's marriage limit with ijma proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maximum number of wives a slave may marry and explain the Hanafi proof from ijma and the principle of half rights.

Core Idea
A slave may marry only two wives, because slavery halves blessings and punishments.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling in Hanafi marriage law: the maximum number of wives a slave can have at one time. While a free man may marry up to four, the slave's capacity is halved to two. The proof stems from the consensus of the Companions and the legal principle that the slave's status is half of a free man's. The commentary in the passage also clarifies that this applies to all types of slaves, including the mudabbar and mukatab, and that the slave's permissible sexual relations are restricted to marriage only, not concubinage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
State the exact ruling: a slave may marry up to two wives. Note that the wives may be free or slave women.
The ruling is a limitation on what would otherwise be the free man's license of four wives.
2
Understand the proof
The proof given is twofold: (1) the consensus of the Companions (ijma), and (2) the principle that slavery is 'half of blessings and punishment' — meaning the slave's legal capacity is halved in many respects.
Ijma is a source of law in Hanafi usul, and here it is cited as the primary evidence.
3
Examine the expansion
The ruling is generalized to all types of slaves (mudabbar, mukatab, ordinary slave). The passage also notes that the slave may not take a concubine; his permissible outlet is restricted to marriage alone.
This is an expansion of the base text (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) to clarify scope and exceptions.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine a mukatab slave who has almost paid for his freedom. He wants to marry three women. Apply the ruling: he cannot marry more than two. Even though he is close to freedom, his current status as a slave binds him.
The ruling applies regardless of the slave's progress toward freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واثنتين للعبد) أي رجل تزوج اثنتين له حرتين كانتا أو أمتين، ولا يجوز أكثر منه في النكاح لإجماع الصحابة، ولأن الرق منصف نعمة وعقوبة. أطلق في العبد فشمل المدبر والمكاتب وقيد بالتزوج لأنه لا يحل له التسري ولا أن يسريه مولاه ولا يملك المكاتب والعبد شيئاً إلا الطلاق ذكره الأسبيجابي. وحاصله في عقد النكاح وملك اليمين ولم يكن الثاني للعبد لأنه لا يملك وإن ملك أن الحل منحصر فانحصر حله في عقد النكاح.
His statement: (and two for the slave) means a man who marries two, whether free women or slave women, and it is not permissible to have more than that in marriage due to the consensus of the Companions, and because slavery is half of blessings and punishment. He made it absolute regarding the slave, so it includes the mudabbar and the mukatab, and he restricted it to marriage because it is not permissible for him to take a concubine, nor for his master to give him a concubine, and the mukatab and slave own nothing except divorce, as mentioned by al-Asbijabi. The gist of it is that in the marriage contract and right hand possession, the second [marriage] is not for the slave because he does not own [a concubine], and even if he owns [a slave woman], the permissible is restricted so his permissible is confined to the marriage contract.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It states that a slave may marry up to two wives, whether they are free or slave women. The evidence is the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, and the reasoning that the slave's rights are half of a free man's. The ruling applies to all types of slaves: the mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) and the mukatab (who has a contract of manumission). The slave is not permitted to take a concubine (sirriyah) because he does not own the right to do so; his master cannot grant him that either. In terms of ownership, the slave can only own the right to divorce, not property for marriage. Therefore, the slave's permissible means of fulfilling desires is confined to marriage alone, not concubinage.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to buy his freedom in installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred until after the master's death.
إجماع الصحابة (ijma al-sahabah): Consensus of the Companions of the Prophet, a source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many wives may a slave marry according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The slave's marriage limit is two wives, established by ijma and the halving principle.

Nikah — Marriage

Istibra' When Marrying One's Own Slave Woman
Ruling on istibra' for a master marrying his own slave woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether istibra' is required when a master marries his own slave woman and explain the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Istibra' for one's own slave: not obligatory for the founding imams, but obligatory for later fatwa.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses a nuanced issue: a master who marries his own female slave after having had intercourse with her. The question is whether he must observe istibra' (a waiting period) before consummating the marriage. The Hanafi school has an internal difference, with the earlier imams allowing intercourse without istibra' and later scholars insisting on it to protect lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a master marrying his own slave woman and then having intercourse with her. The dispute is whether istibra' is required before that intercourse.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi: between the two (Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad, and later resolved by later scholars.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They say it is permissible without istibra' because the marriage itself indicates an empty womb; the slave woman is not a full bed (firash) since lineage is not established without claim.
Key point: they see marriage as a sign (amarah) of emptiness.
3
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani
Muhammad dislikes intercourse without istibra' because of the possibility that the womb is occupied with the master's water, analogizing to the case of purchase where istibra' is required.
He does not say it is obligatory, but rather makruh (disliked) without it.
4
Synthesize the reconciliation from al-Nihayah
The text from al-Nihayah notes that there is no real disagreement: Abu Hanifa did not say it is not recommended, and Muhammad did not say it is obligatory; the difference is on the level of recommendation versus mere permissibility.
This reconciles the views but later scholars moved to obligation.
5
Identify the later fatwa (mufta bihi)
Later scholars like Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi consider istibra' obligatory to protect lineage. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa today.
The marriage itself is valid, but if the master consummates without istibra', he commits a sin according to this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (والموطوءة بملك) أي حل تزوج من وطئها المولى بملك يمين لأنها ليست بفراش لمولاها لأنها لو جاءت بولد لا يثبت نسبه من غير دعوى فلا يلزم الجمع بين الفراشين، وأفاد أنه يحل له وطؤها من غير استبراء وهو قولهما وقال محمد : لا أحب أن يطأها حتى يستبرئها لأنه احتمل الشغل بماء المولى فوجب التنزه كما في الشراء. ولهما أن الحكم بجواز النكاح أمارة الفراغ فلا يؤمر بالاستبراء لا استحباباً ولا وجوباً بخلاف الشراء لأنه يجوز الشغل . كذا في الهداية. وذكر في النهاية أنه لا خلاف بينهم في الحاصل فإن أبا حنيفة قال : للزوج أن يطأها بغير استبراء واجب ولم يقل لا يستحب. ومحمد لم يقل أيضاً هو واجب ولكنه قال : لا أحب له أن يطأها ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأن ما في الهداية من قوله «لا يؤمر به لا استحباباً ولا وجوباً يأبى هذا الحمل ولم يذكر المصنف استبراء المولى. وفي الهداية عليه أن يستبرئها صيانة لمائه. وظاهره الوجوب، وحمله في النهاية والمعراج على الاستحباب دون الحتم. وفي الذخيرة : وإذا أراد الرجل أن يزوج من إنسان وقد كان يطؤها بعض مشايخنا قالوا يستحب له أن يستبرئها بحيضة ثم يزوجها كما لو أراد بيعاً. والصحيح أنه ههنا يجب الاستبراء وإليه مال شمس الأئمة السرخسي وقد جعل الوجوب في الحاوي الحصيري قول محمد أطلق في الموطوءة بالملك فشمل أم الولد ما لم تكن حبلى منه كما قدمناه
His statement: (and the one who has been had intercourse with by right hand possession) means it is permissible for a master to marry a woman he has had intercourse with by right hand possession because she is not a bed (firash) for her master, because if she bore a child, its lineage would not be established without a claim, so there is no necessary combination of two beds. And it indicates that it is permissible for him to have intercourse with her without istibra', and this is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Muhammad said: I do not like that he have intercourse with her until he performs istibra' because of the possibility of being occupied with the master's water, so it is obligatory to avoid it as in the case of purchase. For them (the two), the ruling of permissibility of marriage is a sign of emptiness, so he is not commanded to perform istibra', neither as recommended nor as obligatory, unlike the case of purchase because being occupied is possible. Thus in al-Hidayah. It is mentioned in al-Nihayah that there is no disagreement among them in essence, for Abu Hanifa said: It is permissible for the husband to have intercourse with her without obligatory istibra', and he did not say it is not recommended. Muhammad also did not say it is obligatory, but he said: I do not like that he have intercourse with her. Hmm. There is consideration because what is in al-Hidayah from his statement 'he is not commanded to do it, neither as recommended nor obligatory' contradicts this interpretation, and the author did not mention the istibra' of the master. In al-Hidayah, it is upon him to perform istibra' to protect his water. Its apparent meaning is obligation, and it is interpreted in al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj as recommendation rather than necessity. In al-Dhakhirah: If a man wants to marry [his slave woman] to another person, and he used to have intercourse with her, some of our scholars said: it is recommended for him to perform istibra' with one menstrual period then marry her, just as if he wanted to sell her. The correct view is that istibra' is obligatory here, and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi inclined to that, and obligation was made in al-Hawi al-Husayri as the saying of Muhammad. He made it absolute regarding the one had intercourse with by right hand possession, so it includes umm al-walad as long as she is not pregnant from him, as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses a master who wishes to marry his own slave woman whom he has previously had intercourse with. The question is whether he must wait for istibra' (a waiting period to ensure the womb is empty) before having intercourse with her after the marriage. The position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two, qawluhuma) is that he may do so without istibra', because the very fact that the marriage is permissible indicates the womb is empty (since the former status of concubinage is not a full marriage bed that would establish lineage without claim). Muhammad reported in al-Hidayah dislikes it until istibra', as a precaution. The text reconciles the views, noting that Abu Hanifa did not say istibra' is not recommended, and Muhammad did not say it is obligatory, so the disagreement is on whether it is recommended or just permissible. However, later scholars like al-Sarakhsi say it is obligatory, and that is the correct view when the master intends to marry the slave woman himself. The ruling also applies if he wishes to marry her to another man: most scholars say it is recommended to perform istibra' first, but some say it is obligatory. The passage also clarifies that 'the one had intercourse with by right hand possession' includes umm al-walad (a slave woman who has borne her master's child), as long as she is not currently pregnant from him (in which case marriage is invalid as per the previous lesson).

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period (usually one menstrual cycle) to verify that the womb is not pregnant.
موطوءة بملك (mawtū'a bi-milk): A female slave with whom the master has had sexual intercourse by right of ownership.
أمارة (amārah): A sign or indication that is used as legal evidence, though not definitive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa, what is the ruling on a master who marries his own slave woman with whom he had intercourse, regarding istibra'?
A) Istibra' is not required at all
B) Istibra' is recommended but not obligatory
C) Istibra' is obligatory
D) The marriage itself is invalid

Key Takeaway
Though the two founding imams did not require istibra', the later Hanafi fatwa makes it obligatory to protect lineage when a master marries his own slave woman.

Marrying a Woman Who Committed Zina: Permissibility and Proof
Ruling on marrying a woman who committed zina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a woman known for zina and explain the proof based on abrogation of Quran 24:3.

Core Idea
Marrying an adulteress is permissible because the prohibition is abrogated.

This lesson addresses a common question: may a Muslim man marry a woman who has committed zina? The Hanafi school rules that it is permissible (mubah), contrary to the apparent meaning of Quran 24:3. You will learn the evidence from abrogation and prophetic practice, as well as the nuance regarding istibra'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying a woman who has committed zina. According to the text, it is permissible (mubah). The husband may have intercourse without istibra', though Imam Muhammad dislikes that.
Note the distinction between permissibility of marriage and recommendation regarding intercourse.
2
Locate the dalil for permissibility
The primary proof is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed a man to keep his wife despite her immodesty. This indicates that marriage to such a woman is not inherently forbidden.
The hadith is ahad, so its dalala is zanni, but it is supported by the principle of abrogation.
3
Understand the abrogation argument
Quran 24:3 apparently forbids believers from marrying fornicators. However, this verse is abrogated (mansukh) by the general command in Quran 4:3 to marry what is good. Hanafi usul accepts abrogation of Quran by Quran.
Abrogation requires that the later verse (4:3, revealed in Madinah) override the earlier one (24:3, Mekkan).
4
Note the ikhtilaf and intra-madhhab nuance
The Shafi'i school considers such marriages valid but makruh. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Muhammad recommends istibra' before intercourse, but the relied-upon position is that it is not required.
The mufta bihi is permissibility without required istibra'.
5
Apply the ruling in practice
A man may validly marry a woman who has committed zina, and the marriage is not sinful. However, if the woman persists in zina, it may be makruh tanzih.
This ruling does not excuse the sin of zina itself; it only addresses the permissibility of marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو زنا) أي وحل تزوج الموطوءة بالزنا أي الزانية لو رأى امرأة تزني فتزوجها جاز وللزوج أن يطأها بغير استبراء. وقال محمد: لا أحب له أن يطأها من غير استبراء. وهذا صريح في جواز تزوج الزانية . وأما قوله تعالى والزانية لا ينكحها إلا زان أو مشرك وحرم ذلك على المؤمنين ) [النور: ٣] فمنسوخ بقوله تعالى فانكحوا ما طاب لكم ﴾ [النساء : ٣] على ما قيل بدليل الحديث أن رجلا أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : يا رسول الله إن امرأتي لا تدفع يد لامس فقال عليه السلام: طلقها. فقال: إني أحبها وهي جميلة . فقال عليه السلام استمتع بها.
His statement: (or zina) means it is permissible to marry a woman who has been had intercourse with by zina, i.e., the adulteress, if a man sees a woman committing zina and marries her, it is permissible, and the husband may have intercourse with her without istibra'. Muhammad said: I do not like that he have intercourse with her without istibra'. This is explicit regarding the permissibility of marrying the adulteress. As for the saying of Allah Most High: 'The adulteress – none marries her except an adulterer or a polytheist, and that is forbidden for the believers' [Quran 24:3], it is abrogated by His saying: 'So marry what is good for you' [Quran 4:3] according to what has been said, evidenced by the hadith that a man came to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, my wife does not repel the hand of a toucher.' He said: 'Divorce her.' The man said: 'But I love her and she is beautiful.' He said: 'Enjoy her.'
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that it is permissible to marry a woman known to have committed zina (fornication). The husband may have intercourse with her without istibra', although Muhammad recommends istibra' as a precaution. The apparent meaning of Quran 24:3 forbids believers from marrying such women, but this verse is considered abrogated (mansukh) by the general command to marry what is good (Quran 4:3). The proof cited is a hadith: a man complained about his wife's immodesty, and the Prophet advised divorce, but when the man expressed love for her, the Prophet permitted him to keep her. This indicates that marriage to a woman of immoral conduct is not inherently forbidden. The Hanafi position thus permits such marriages, though it may be discouraged if the woman persists in sin.

Key Terms
منسوخ (mansukh): Abrogated; referring to the ruling of Quran 24:3 being replaced by Quran 4:3.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period to ensure the womb is clear of pregnancy; in this context, recommended before intercourse with a previously adulterous woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What evidence is used in the text to support the permissibility of marrying an adulteress?
A) Quran 24:3 itself
B) Abrogation of Quran 24:3 by Quran 4:3 and a hadith
C) Ijma of the Sahaba
D) Qiyas on the permissibility of marrying a fornicator

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits marrying a woman who committed zina because the relevant Quranic prohibition is abrogated, as supported by prophetic practice.

Master Marrying Pregnant Slave with Acknowledgement
Ruling on master marrying pregnant slave with or without acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit acknowledgment and implied denial of paternity when a master marries his pregnant slave, and apply the ruling that the marriage itself is valid.

Core Idea
Explicit acknowledgment by the master affirms lineage; silence implies denial of the child.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding a master who marries his own pregnant slave-concubine. The key question is whether the act of marriage itself implies acceptance or denial of the child's paternity. The Hanafi position hinges on whether the master explicitly acknowledges the pregnancy or remains silent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the master's statement
The passage contrasts two cases: the master explicitly says 'she is pregnant from me' or does not acknowledge the pregnancy at all.
The master's statement is the key variable.
2
Determine the ruling on marriage validity
In both cases, the marriage is valid (sahih). The passage uses 'ينبغي أن يجوز النكاح' meaning it is appropriate that it is permissible.
The focus is on the effect on lineage, not the marriage itself.
3
Assess the effect on lineage
If the master acknowledges, the explicit statement overrides any implication, so the child's lineage is affirmed. If he does not acknowledge, the act of marriage serves as an implied denial (نفي دلالة).
Implied denial is a recognized legal concept in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the proof for implied denial
The passage cites the case of a slave woman with three children where the master claims only the eldest. That child's lineage is established, and the others are denied by implication due to his selective claim.
This analogy supports that actions (like marriage without acknowledgment) can negate lineage.
5
Apply the principle to other scenarios
Understand that in Hanafi fiqh, both explicit statements and certain actions can negate lineage. The principle is that lineage is established by certainty and can only be negated by certainty or strong implication.
This ruling is specific to the context of master and slave; different rules apply to free persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح فصل في المحرمات
الهداية. وظاهره أن المولى اعترف بأن الحمل منه لأنه قال وهي حامل منه فلذا لم يكن تزويجه إياها نفياً للولد دلالة لأن الصريح بخلافه . فلو لم يعترف به وزوجها وهي حامل ينبغي أن يجوز النكاح ويكون نفياً دلالة فإن النسب كما ينتفى بالصريح ينتفي بالدلالة بدليل مسألة الأمة جاءت بأولاد ثلاثة فادعى المولى أكبرهم حيث يثبت نسبه وينتفي نسب غيره بدلالة اقتصاره على البعض كما في فتح القدير.
The Hidayah. Its apparent meaning is that the master acknowledged that the pregnancy is from him because he said 'and she is pregnant from him.' Therefore his marrying her was not a denial of the child by implication, because explicit statement is contrary to that. If he did not acknowledge it and married her while she was pregnant, it is appropriate that the marriage is valid and it is a denial by implication. Indeed lineage is negated by explicit statement as it is negated by implication, as evidenced by the case of a slave woman who gave birth to three children and the master claimed the eldest — his lineage is established and the others' lineage is negated by implication due to his limitation to some, as in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the ruling when a master marries his pregnant slave-concubine. If the master explicitly says 'she is pregnant from me,' then the marriage does not imply denial of the child, because the explicit acknowledgement overrides any implication. If he does not acknowledge, but simply marries her while pregnant, the marriage itself is valid but acts as an implied denial of the child's paternity. This is because lineage can be negated by implication just as by explicit statement. The proof cited is a case from Fath al-Qadir: a slave woman has three children; the master claims only the eldest — that child's lineage is confirmed while the others are negated implicitly by his selective claim.

Key Terms
نفي الولد دلالة (nafy al-walad dilālah): Implied negation of paternity based on actions rather than explicit statement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master marries his pregnant slave without acknowledging the pregnancy, what is the ruling regarding the child's lineage?
A) The child's lineage is established because marriage implies acceptance
B) The child's lineage is negated by implication
C) The child's lineage is suspended until the master makes a statement
D) The marriage is invalid because of the uncertainty

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a master marries his pregnant slave, the effect on the child's lineage depends on whether the master explicitly acknowledges the pregnancy; silence implies denial.

Marrying a Woman Who Committed Zina: Hanafi Position on Validity and Istibra'
Ruling on validity of marriage to a zaniah and the issue of istibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a woman known to have committed zina, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on istibra', and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument using the abrogation argument.

Core Idea
Marrying a zaniah is valid; intercourse is permissible without istibra'—the Prophet said 'enjoy her.'

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a sensitive issue: can a Muslim man marry a woman he knows committed zina? The Hanafi school answers with a clear yes, and further permits intercourse without waiting for istibra'. But this ruling is contested by other madhhabs and even within the Hanafi school itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
The act is marrying a woman known to have committed zina. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) and intercourse is allowed without istibra'.
The text explicitly states 'jaza' and 'yati'uha bighayri istibra'.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani disliked (la yuhibbu) intercourse without istibra', but did not prohibit it. This is a preference, not a legal ruling.
This is an example of an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on preference.
3
Analyze the apparent objection from Quran 24:3
The verse 'The adulteress is not married except by an adulterer or a polytheist' seems to forbid marrying a zaniah. The Hanafi response is that this verse is abrogated (mansukh) by the general permission in Quran 4:3 to marry lawful women.
Abrogation (naskh) is a key tool in Hanafi usul for reconciling conflicting texts.
4
Examine the Hanafi evidence: the hadith
The Prophet told a man whose wife was promiscuous to 'enjoy her' (istamti' biha) when the man expressed love for her. This shows it is permissible to remain married to a fornicator.
The hadith is ahad, but it is sufficient for establishing permissibility.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf with other schools
Malikis and Shafi'is require repentance for the marriage to be valid or consider it makruh. The practical difference is that Hanafis do not require istibra' or repentance, while others may require them.
This difference affects the validity of past marriages and future contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو زنا) أي وحل تزوج الموطوءة بالزنا أي الزانية لو رأى امرأة تزني فتزوجها جاز وللزوج أن يطأها بغير استبراء. وقال محمد: لا أحب له أن يطأها من غير استبراء. وهذا صريح في جواز تزوج الزانية . وأما قوله تعالى والزانية لا ينكحها إلا زان أو مشرك وحرم ذلك على المؤمنين ) [النور: ٣] فمنسوخ بقوله تعالى فانكحوا ما طاب لكم ﴾ [النساء : ٣] على ما قيل بدليل الحديث أن رجلا أتى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال : يا رسول الله إن امرأتي لا تدفع يد لامس فقال عليه السلام: طلقها. فقال: إني أحبها وهي جميلة . فقال عليه السلام استمتع بها. وفي المجتبى من آخر الحظر والإباحة لا يجب على الزوج تطليق الفاجرة ولا عليها تسريح الفاجر إلا إذا خافا أن لا يقيما حدود الله فلا بأس أن يتفرقا ا هـ.
His statement: (or zina) i.e., it is permissible to marry the one who was penetrated by zina, meaning the adulteress. If a man sees a woman committing zina and then marries her, it is permissible and the husband may have intercourse with her without istibra'. Muhammad said: I do not like him to have intercourse with her without istibra'. This is explicit in the permissibility of marrying the adulteress. As for the verse 'The adulteress is not married except by an adulterer or a polytheist, and that is forbidden to the believers' [24:3], it is abrogated by the verse 'Marry such women as are lawful for you' [4:3] according to what is said, evidenced by the hadith that a man came to the Prophet saying, 'O Messenger of Allah, my wife does not repel the hand of a toucher.' He said, 'Divorce her.' He said, 'I love her and she is beautiful.' He said, 'Enjoy her.' In Al-Mujtaba, at the end of the chapter of prohibition and permission: It is not obligatory for the husband to divorce the sinful woman, nor for her to divorce the sinful husband, unless they fear they cannot uphold the limits of Allah, in which case there is no harm in separating.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage rules on marrying a woman who has committed zina (adultery). The Hanafi position is that such a marriage is valid, and the husband may have intercourse without waiting for istibra'. Muhammad al-Shaybani again expresses a preference for istibra' but does not deem it obligatory. The text then addresses the potential objection from the Quranic verse (24:3) that seems to forbid marrying a zaniah except to a fornicator or polytheist. The answer is that this verse is abrogated by the general permission in Surah al-Nisa' (4:3) to marry lawful women. A hadith supports this: a man complained that his wife was promiscuous, and the Prophet neither commanded divorce nor forbade keeping her; he said 'enjoy her' (i.e., keep her). Thus, marrying a fornicator is permissible, and while there is a moral recommendation to separate if one fears sin, it is not obligatory.

Key Terms
زانية (zāniyah): A woman who has committed illicit sexual intercourse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a man marrying a woman known to have committed zina?
A) Valid without istibra'
B) Valid only with istibra'
C) Invalid
D) Makruh tahrimi

Key Takeaway
Marriage to a zaniah is valid and intercourse is permitted without istibra', based on the hadith of the Prophet.

Marrying a Lawful Woman Alongside a Prohibited Woman: Mahr Distribution
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr with rulings and analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mahr distribution when a lawful and prohibited woman are married in one contract, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A prohibited woman cannot be a valid object of a marriage contract — her inclusion is laghw, so all mahr goes to the lawful wife.

This passage examines a specific scenario in marriage law: a man contracts to marry two women simultaneously, one lawful and one prohibited (e.g., a mahram, a married woman, or a polytheist). The central question is how the named mahr (dower) is distributed. Abu Hanifa and his two eminent disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagree on the reasoning and outcome. Understanding this dispute reveals foundational principles about eligibility (mahalliyah) and the effect of void conditions in marriage contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
Recognize that the act is a single marriage contract covering two women, one lawful and one prohibited. The dispute is about the distribution of the named mahr: does the lawful woman receive all of it, or is it divided proportionally?
The contract itself is valid for the lawful woman per Abu Hanifa; the prohibited woman's inclusion is null.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Its Proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the entire named mahr goes to the lawful woman. His proof: the prohibited woman lacks mahalliyah (eligibility to be a wife), so her inclusion is laghw (null and void) — like joining a wall to a marriage contract. Therefore no mahr is allocated to her.
The analogy with a sale of a free man and a slave together: the slave part is invalid, and the price goes entirely to the free man.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Proof
The Sahibayn hold that the mahr is divided proportionally based on the mahr mithl (dower of equals) of each woman. Their proof: the named mahr corresponds to both instances of sexual access (bud'ayn) mentioned in the contract, so it should be divided, analogously to selling two slaves where one is a mudabbar.
In their example, if the named mahr is 1000, lawful woman's mahr mithl is 1000, prohibited's is 2000, then the lawful gets 333.33 and the rest is dropped.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to the Sahibayn's Analogies
The text rebuts the Sahibayn's analogies: (1) The mudabbar slave is partially eligible as property, so he enters the contract for division, but a prohibited woman has zero eligibility. (2) In the case of addressing two women with an offer, both are eligible, so division happens; here the prohibited woman is ineligible from the start.
Thus Abu Hanifa's view is preferred (tarjih).
5
Examine the Two Narrations on Intercourse with the Prohibited Woman
If the husband had intercourse with the prohibited woman, there are two narrations within Abu Hanifa's school: (a) she receives mahr mithl capped at her share of the named mahr; (b) she receives full mahr mithl (the more correct view). This nuance shows that the form of the contract may create a semblance (shubha) that obligates mahr and drops the hadd penalty.
This does not contradict the principle that her inclusion is laghw; it is a separate ruling based on actual consummation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمضمومة إلى محرمة) أي وحل نكاح امرأة محللة ضمت إلى امرأة محرمة كأن عقد على امرأتين إحداهما محرمة أو ذات زوج أو وثنية بخلاف ما إذا جمع بين حر وعبد في البيع حيث لا يصح في العبد لأن قبول العقد في الحر شرط فاسد في بيع العبد وهنا المبطل يخص المحرمة والنكاح لا يبطل بالشرط الفاسد.
قوله : (والمسمى (لها أي جميع المسمى للمحللة المضمومة إلى محرمة عند أبي حنيفة نظراً إلى أن ضم المحرمة في عقد النكاح لغو كضم الجدار لعدم المحلية والانقسام من حكم المساواة في الدخول في العقد، ولم يجبت الحد بوطء المحرمة لأن سقوطه من حكم صورة العقد لا من حكم انعقاده، فليس قوله بعدم الانقسام بناء على أن عدم الدخول في العقد مناف لقوله بسقوط الحد» لوجود صورة العقد كما قد توهم كما لا يخفى وعندهما يقسم على مهر مثليهما كان يكون المسمى ألفاً ومهر مثل المحرمة ألفان والمحللة ألف فيلزم ثلاث مائة وثلاث وثلاثون وثلث درهم للمحللة ويسقط الباقي نظراً إلى أن المسمى قوبل بالبضعين فينقسم عليهما كما لو جمع بين عبدين فإذا أحدهما مدبر، وكما إذا خاطب امرأتين بالنكاح بألف فأجابت إحداهما دون الأخرى وأجيب عن الأول بأن المدبر محل في الجملة لكونه مالاً فدخل تحت الانعقاد فانقسم بخلاف المحرمة لعدم المحلية أصلاً. وعن الثاني بأنهما استويا في الدخول تحت الإيجاب للمحلية فانقسم المهر عليهما فترجح قوله على قولهما. وأراد على قوله ما لو دخل بالمحرمة فإن فيه روايتين : في رواية الزيادات يلزمه مهر مثلها لا يجاوز به حصتها من المسمى ومقتضاه الدخول في العقد وإلا لوجب مهر المثل بالغاً ما بلغ، وجوابه أن المنع من المجاوزة على ما خصها من المسمى يحصل بمجرد التسمية ورضاها بالقدر المسمى لا بانعقاد العقد عليها ودخولها تحته وذلك موجود في المحرمة. وفي رواية أخرى يجب مهر المثل بالغاً ما بلغ وهو الأصح كما في المبسوط، ومقتضاه الدخول في العقد وقد قال بعدمه وهو يقتضي أجنبيتها عنه فلا يجب مهر المثل لأنه فرع الدخول في عقد فاسد، وجوابه أن وجوبه بالعذر الذي وجب به درء الحد وهو صورة العقد وأورد على قولهما أيضاً كيف وجب لها حصتها من الألف بالدخول وهو حكم دخولها في العقد، ثم يجب الحد ولا يجتمع والمهر ولا مخلص إلا يتخصيصهما الدعوى فيجب الحد لانتفاء شبهة الحل والمهر للانقسام بالدخول في العقد
His statement: (And the one joined to a prohibited woman) i.e., it is permissible to marry a lawful woman who is joined in the contract with a prohibited woman, such as contracting for two women one of whom is prohibited (e.g., a mahram), or a married woman, or a polytheist. This is unlike the case of combining a free man and a slave in a sale, where the slave part is invalid because acceptance of the contract in a free man is a void condition in the sale of a slave. Here the invalidating factor is specific to the prohibited woman, and marriage is not invalidated by a void condition.
His statement: (And the named [mahr] is for her) i.e., all of the named mahr is for the lawful woman who is joined to a prohibited woman according to Abu Hanifa, considering that joining the prohibited woman in a marriage contract is null (laghw) like joining a wall, because she is not eligible for marriage, and division is a consequence of equal participation in the contract. And hadd penalty is not required for intercourse with the prohibited woman because its dropping is due to the form of the contract, not its effectiveness. So his statement that there is no division is not based on her not entering the contract, which would contradict his statement that the hadd is dropped due to the existence of the form of the contract, as might be imagined. According to the two (Sahibayn), the mahr is divided based on the mahr mithl of both. For example, if the named mahr is 1000, and the mahr mithl of the prohibited woman is 2000 and that of the lawful woman is 1000, then 333 1/3 dirhams are due to the lawful woman and the rest is dropped. This is because the named mahr corresponds to both instances of sexual access (bud'ayn), so it is divided between them, as when two slaves are sold together and one is a mudabbar, or when a man addresses two women with a marriage offer of 1000 and one responds and the other does not. The response to the first analogy: the mudabbar is partially eligible as property, so he enters the contract and division is made, unlike the prohibited woman who has no eligibility at all. To the second: both women equally enter the offer due to eligibility, so the mahr is divided between them. Thus Abu Hanifa's view is preferred over the two's. Regarding the case where he has intercourse with the prohibited woman, there are two narrations: in the narration of al-Ziyadat, he is liable for her mahr mithl but not exceeding her share of the named mahr; this implies she entered the contract, otherwise full mahr mithl would be due. The answer: the restriction to the named share is due to the mere naming and her consent to that amount, not because of the contract's validity on her. In another narration, full mahr mithl is due, which is the more correct view as in al-Mabsut; this implies she entered the contract, yet Abu Hanifa said she did not, which would mean she is a stranger and no mahr is due because mahr is a result of an invalid contract. The answer: its obligation is due to the excuse that prevented the hadd, i.e., the form of the contract. An objection to the two: how can she be entitled to a share of the 1000 by intercourse, which is a ruling of entering the contract, and then the hadd be imposed? They cannot combine; the only solution is to specify the claim: the hadd is due because the semblance of legality is absent, and the mahr is due by division due to entering the contract.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage deals with a contract where a man marries two women in one contract, one lawful and one prohibited (like a mahram, a married woman, or a polytheist). According to Abu Hanifa, the marriage to the lawful woman is valid, and she receives the entire named mahr, because the prohibited woman's inclusion is null (laghw) — she is not eligible for marriage at all, so the mahr is not divided. However, if the man had intercourse with the prohibited woman, some rulings apply: in one narration, she gets mahr mithl capped at her share of the named mahr; in another, full mahr mithl is due, which is the correct view. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the mahr is divided proportionally based on the mahr mithl of each woman, because the named mahr corresponds to both instances of sexual access. Abu Hanifa's argument is stronger by analogy: just as in sale, if a free man and slave are sold together, the slave part is invalid, and the money goes entirely to the free man. The text discusses objections and answers, showing the internal Hanafi debate.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Null and void; having no effect in the contract
محلية (maḥallīyah): Eligibility to be the object of a marriage contract
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred until the owner's death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a man marries two women in one contract — one lawful and one prohibited — how is the mahr distributed?
A) The mahr is divided equally between both women
B) The entire named mahr goes to the lawful woman
C) The mahr is divided proportionally by mahr mithl
D) The contract is entirely invalid so no mahr is owing

Key Takeaway
When a lawful and prohibited woman are married in one contract, the lawful woman receives the entire named mahr per the preferred Hanafi view; the prohibited woman's inclusion is legally null.

Invalidity of Temporary Marriage: Mut'ah and Muwaqqat
Ruling that temporary marriage is invalid, with distinction between mut'ah and muwaqqat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on temporary marriages and distinguish between nikah al-mut'ah and nikah al-muwaqqat.

Core Idea
Any marriage with a fixed term is invalid (batil) in Hanafi fiqh, regardless of wording or witnesses.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical point in Hanafi marriage law: not only is the well-known 'mut'ah' (pleasure marriage) invalid, but also any marriage contract that includes a time limit, even if the words of permanent marriage are used. The distinction between the two is mainly in wording and the presence of witnesses, but both are equally void. Understanding this ruling helps you grasp the Hanafi insistence on permanent, unqualified marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage declares both nikah al-mut'ah and nikah al-muwaqqat as invalid (batil). This is the core takeaway.
Invalid means the contract is void from the start, producing no legal effects.
2
Define each type
Mut'ah uses words of enjoyment like 'atamatta'u biki' or 'astamti''. Muwaqqat uses the words of permanent marriage (nikah/tazwij) but with a specified time limit.
The wording is the initial distinguishing feature.
3
Note the distinction in al-'Inayah
Al-'Inayah adds that muwaqqat also requires witnesses and a stated period, while mut'ah may lack these. However, both are still batil.
Witnesses do not salvage a time-limited contract.
4
Conclude the core cause
The essential cause of invalidity is the time limitation itself. Whether the contract is called mut'ah or muwaqqat, any fixation of a term makes it void.
This principle is unique to Hanafi fiqh in its strictness against any temporal condition in marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبطل نكاح المتعة والموقت وفرق بينهما في النهاية والمعراج بأن يذكر في الموقت لفظ النكاح أو التزويج مع التوقيت وفي المتعة لفظ «أتمتع بك» أو «أستمتع». وفي العناية بفرق آخر أن الموقت يكون بحضرة الشهود ويذكر فيه مدة معينة
الحد
قوله : (وجوابه أن المنع من المجاوزة إلى آخر كلامه لم يتضح لنا المرام في هذا المقام فعليك بالتأمل والمراجعة قوله: (وفي العناية بفرق (آخر حاصله أن التمتع ما اشتمل على مادة متعة . مع عدم
His statement: (And invalid is the marriage of mut'ah and the time-limited marriage). The difference between them in al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj is: in muwaqqat, the word 'nikah' or 'tazwij' is mentioned with a time limit; in mut'ah, the phrase 'I enjoy you' (atamatta'u biki) or 'I seek enjoyment' (astamti'). In al-'Inayah, another difference: muwaqqat is witnessed and a specific period is mentioned.
The hadd (limit)
His statement: (And its answer that the prevention from exceeding... until the end of his speech is not clear to us, so you must reflect and refer. His statement: (In al-'Inayah another difference: essentially that tamattu' includes the root of mut'ah... with absence.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage declares that both nikkah al-mut'ah (pleasure marriage) and nikkah al-muwaqqat (time-limited marriage) are invalid (batil). The distinction between them is explained: in muwaqqat, the words of marriage (nikah or tazwij) are used along with a time limit; in mut'ah, the words of enjoyment (tamattu' or istimta') are used. Another distinction from al-'Inayah is that muwaqqat involves witnesses and a specific fixed period, while mut'ah may not. The text then seems to include a marginal comment from the commentator expressing difficulty with a previous argument and advising the reader to reflect. The ruling is clear: any marriage with a fixed term is invalid, whether called mut'ah or muwaqqat.

Key Terms
نكاح المتعة (nikāḥ al-mut'ah): A marriage contracted for a temporary period using the word 'tamattu' or similar
نكاح الموقت (nikāḥ al-muwaqqat): A marriage with a fixed time period but using the words of permanent marriage (nikah/tazwij)

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Invalid (batil) because it is a time-limited marriage
C) Valid as long as the mahr is given
D) Permissible with the intention of later divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, any marriage with a fixed term is absolutely invalid, regardless of wording or witnesses.

Dower When Lawful and Prohibited Women Are Combined in One Contract
Explaining the ruling on dower when a lawful and prohibited woman are married in one contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on dower distribution when a lawful and prohibited woman are married in one contract, explain the rebuttal of the Sahibayn's analogy, and identify the practical consequence.

Core Idea
The prohibited woman's inclusion is void, so the lawful woman takes all the named dower.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of the named dower (mahr musamma) when a lawful woman and a prohibited woman are combined in a single marriage contract. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the entire dower goes to the lawful woman, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for proportional division based on mahr al-mithl. We will analyze the proofs, analogies, and rebuttals to understand the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does Abu Hanifa rule regarding the dower when a lawful woman is combined with a prohibited woman in one marriage contract?
The answer is that the entire named dower goes to the lawful woman.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which two scholars oppose Abu Hanifa and what do they argue?
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that the dower should be divided proportionally according to the mahr al-mithl of each woman.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What analogies do the Sahibayn use to support their view?
They analogize to combining a free person and a slave in a sale, or addressing a marriage offer to two women where one accepts.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the commentator refute the Sahibayn's analogies?
The mudabbar slave has partial legal capacity as property, unlike the prohibited woman who has none. In the two-women example, both have legal capacity and enter the contract equally, unlike the prohibited woman.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions?
Under Abu Hanifa, the lawful woman receives all the named dower. Under the Sahibayn, she receives only a portion and the rest is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمضمومة إلى محرمة) أي وحل نكاح امرأة محللة ضمت إلى امرأة محرمة كأن عقد على امرأتين إحداهما محرمة أو ذات زوج أو وثنية بخلاف ما إذا جمع بين حر وعبد في البيع حيث لا يصح في العبد لأن قبول العقد في الحر شرط فاسد في بيع العبد وهنا المبطل يخص المحرمة والنكاح لا يبطل بالشرط الفاسد. قوله : (والمسمى (لها أي جميع المسمى للمحللة المضمومة إلى محرمة عند أبي حنيفة نظراً إلى أن ضم المحرمة في عقد النكاح لغو كضم الجدار لعدم المحلية والانقسام من حكم المساواة في الدخول في العقد، ولم يجب الحد بوطء المحرمة لأن سقوطه من حكم صورة العقد لا من حكم انعقاده، فليس قوله بعدم الانقسام بناء على أن عدم الدخول في العقد مناف لقوله بسقوط الحد لوجود صورة العقد كما قد توهم كما لا يخفى وعندهما يقسم على مهر مثليهما كان يكون المسمى ألفاً ومهر مثل المحرمة ألفان والمحللة ألف فيلزم ثلاث مائة وثلاث وثلاثون وثلث درهم للمحللة ويسقط الباقي نظراً إلى أن المسمى قوبل بالبضعين فينقسم عليهما كما لو جمع بين عبدين فإذا أحدهما مدبر، وكما إذا خاطب امرأتين بالنكاح بألف فأجابت إحداهما دون الأخرى وأجيب عن الأول بأن المدبر محل في الجملة لكونه مالاً فدخل تحت الانعقاد فانقسم بخلاف المحرمة لعدم المحلية أصلاً. وعن الثاني بأنهما استويا في الدخول تحت الإيجاب للمحلية فانقسم المهر عليهما فترجح قوله على قولهما. وأراد على قوله ما لو دخل بالمحرمة فإن فيه روايتين : في رواية الزيادات يلزمه مهر مثلها لا يجاوز به حصتها من المسمى ومقتضاه الدخول في العقد وإلا لوجب مهر المثل بالغاً ما بلغ، وجوابه أن المنع من المجاوزة على ما خصها من المسمى يحصل بمجرد التسمية ورضاها بالقدر المسمى لا بانعقاد العقد عليها ودخولها تحته وذلك موجود في المحرمة. وفي رواية أخرى يجب مهر المثل بالغاً ما بلغ وهو الأصح كما في المبسوط، ومقتضاه الدخول في العقد وقد قال بعدمه وهو يقتضي أجنبيتها عنه فلا يجب مهر المثل لأنه فرع الدخول في عقد فاسد، وجوابه أن وجوبه بالعذر الذي وجب به درء الحد وهو صورة العقد وأورد على قولهما أيضاً كيف وجب لها حصتها من الألف بالدخول وهو حكم دخولها في العقد، ثم يجب الحد ولا يجتمع والمهر ولا مخلص إلا يتخصيصهما الدعوى فيجب الحد لانتفاء شبهة الحل والمهر للانقسام بالدخول في العقد
His statement: 'and the woman combined with a prohibited woman' means that it is permissible to marry a lawful woman who is combined (in one contract) with a prohibited woman, such as contracting with two women, one of whom is prohibited, or a married woman, or an idolatress. Unlike if a free person and a slave are combined in a sale, where the sale is invalid for the slave because the acceptance of the contract for the free person is a void condition in the sale of the slave. Here, the invalidating factor pertains specifically to the prohibited woman, and marriage is not invalidated by a void condition. His statement: 'and the named dower is for her' means all of the named dower goes to the lawful woman who is combined with the prohibited woman according to Abu Hanifa, considering that the inclusion of the prohibited woman in the marriage contract is null and void, like the inclusion of a wall, because she lacks legal capacity (for marriage) and division (of the dower) is based on equal participation in the contract. And the hadd (punishment for fornication) is not incurred by intercourse with the prohibited woman because its non-application is a ruling of the form of the contract, not a ruling of its actual formation. So his statement about non-division is not based on her not entering the contract, which would contradict his statement about the non-application of hadd due to the existence of the form of the contract, as might be assumed. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the dower is divided according to their respective mahr al-mithl (proper dowers). For example, if the named dower is one thousand (dirhams), and the mahr al-mithl of the prohibited woman is two thousand and that of the lawful woman is one thousand, then the lawful woman receives three hundred and thirty-three and one-third dirhams, and the remainder is dropped. This is because the named dower is in exchange for the two sexual rights, so it is divided between them, as if one combined two slaves one of whom is a mudabbar (partially free), and as if one addresses two women with a marriage offer for one thousand and one accepts and the other does not. The response to the first (example) is that the mudabbar is legally capable (of being property) in some sense, so he enters the contract and the price is divided, unlike the prohibited woman who has no legal capacity at all. To the second (example), the response is that both women are equal in entering under the offer due to legal capacity, so the dower is divided between them. Thus, his (Abu Hanifa's) view is preferred over theirs. He intended by his view the case where he had intercourse with the prohibited woman, concerning which there are two narrations: In the narration of al-Ziyadat, he must pay her mahr al-mithl not exceeding her share of the named dower. This implies her entry into the contract, otherwise the full mahr al-mithl would be obligatory. The response is that the restriction of not exceeding her share of the named dower is achieved by the mere naming and her consent to the named amount, not by the conclusion of the contract on her and her entry under it, and that exists in the prohibited woman. In another narration, the full mahr al-mithl is obligatory without limit, and this is the more correct as in al-Mabsut. This implies her entry into the contract, yet he (Abu Hanifa) denied that, which would imply she is a stranger to the contract, so the mahr al-mithl would not be obligatory because it is a branch of entering a void contract. The response is that its obligation is due to the excuse that necessitates the repelling of the hadd, which is the form of the contract. It was also objected against the two: how can she be entitled to her share of the thousand due to intercourse, which is a ruling of her entry into the contract, then the hadd is obligatory? And the mahr and hadd cannot coexist. There is no escape except to specify their claim: the hadd is obligatory because there is no semblance of permissibility, and the mahr is for division due to entry into the contract.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of the named dower (mahr musamma) when a lawful woman (muhallala) is combined in a single marriage contract with a prohibited woman (muharrama). The base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states 'the named dower is for her,' and the commentator explains that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the entire dower goes to the lawful woman. Abu Hanifa argues that the inclusion of the prohibited woman is legally null and void because she lacks legal capacity (mahalliyyah) for the contract; her inclusion is like adding a wall to a sale—it has no effect. The dower is not divided because the prohibited woman does not validly enter the contract. This is supported by the fact that if the man had intercourse with the prohibited woman, the hadd (punishment for fornication) is not applied, due to the form (surah) of the contract existing, not due to its valid formation. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) disagree: they say the dower must be divided proportionally according to the proper dowers (mahr al-mithl) of the two women. They analogize to combining a free person and a slave in a sale, or addressing a marriage offer to two women where one accepts. The commentator then refutes these analogies: a mudabbar slave has partial legal capacity as property, unlike a prohibited woman who has none; and in the case of two women addressed, both have legal capacity and equally enter the contract, whereas the prohibited woman does not. Thus Abu Hanifa's view is preferred. Two narrations are reported regarding the dower if intercourse occurred with the prohibited woman: in one, he pays her mahr al-mithl capped by her share of the named dower; in the other (the more correct), he pays her full mahr al-mithl. The commentator resolves apparent contradictions by referring to the form of the contract as the basis for both the hadd dropping and the mahr obligation. The practical outcome is that under Abu Hanifa the lawful woman takes all the named dower, while under the Sahibayn she takes only a portion.

Key Terms
المحرمة (al-muharrama): A woman whom it is permanently prohibited to marry, such as a mahram relative, or a woman already married, or an idolatress.
المسمى (al-musamma): The dower specifically named and agreed upon in the marriage contract.
الضم (al-damm): Combining two women in a single marriage contract, one lawful and one prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa), when a lawful woman and a prohibited woman are married in one contract with a named dower, what is the distribution?
A) The named dower is divided proportionally based on mahr al-mithl.
B) The lawful woman receives the entire named dower.
C) Prohibited woman receives half the named dower.
D) The contract is void, so no dower is paid.

Key Takeaway
When a lawful and prohibited woman are combined in one marriage contract, the entire named dower goes to the lawful woman according to the preferred Hanafi view.

The Prohibition of Mut'ah and Muwaqqat: Evidence and Consensus
Stating the ruling and primary evidence for prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mut'ah and muwaqqat and explain the primary evidence (ijma' of the Companions and the hadith in Sahih Muslim) that establishes the prohibition.

Core Idea
Mut'ah and muwaqqat are invalid because they contradict the permanence of marriage, prohibited by consensus and explicit hadith.

Marriage in Islam is intended for permanence and establishing lineage. The Hanafi school rigorously upholds this principle, declaring any form of time-limited marriage as invalid. This lesson focuses on the core ruling and the decisive evidence that grounds it, including the consensus of the Companions and a clear Prophetic prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, note that the passage explicitly rules that both mut'ah (temporary marriage) and muwaqqat (time-bound marriage) are invalid (bāṭil) and prohibited (ḥarām).
The ruling is absolute—no exceptions for short or long durations.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The passage cites the ijma' (consensus) of the Companions as transmitted in al-Hidayah, and a specific hadith from Sahih Muslim: 'I had permitted you enjoyment of women, and Allah has prohibited that until the Day of Judgment.'
The hadith is explicit and definitive in meaning (dalāla qaṭ‘ī).
3
Understand the abrogation narrative
Mut'ah was initially permitted and then abrogated twice. The prohibition is permanent. This is reinforced by the statement that three things were abrogated twice: mut'ah, domestic donkey meat, and facing Jerusalem.
The abrogation establishes that the ruling is not open to revival.
4
Handle the dissenting opinions
The passage addresses the opinion of Ibn Abbas (who retracted) and Zafar (who permitted muwaqqat). Both are refuted: Ibn Abbas retracted, and Zafar's view is defeated by the consensus.
The Hanafi relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is prohibition.
5
Apply the ruling to all time-bound forms
The verification from Fath al-Qadir is that muwaqqat is a subset of mut'ah. Therefore, any marriage with a temporal condition—whether explicitly stated or implied—is invalid.
This includes very long periods (e.g., 200 years) and unknown periods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبطل نكاح المتعة والموقت وفرق بينهما في النهاية والمعراج بأن يذكر في الموقت لفظ النكاح أو التزويج مع التوقيت وفي المتعة لفظ «أتمتع بك» أو «أستمتع». وفي العناية بفرق آخر أن الموقت يكون بحضرة الشهود ويذكر فيه مدة معينة بخلاف المتعة فإنه لو قال «أتمتع بك» ولم يذكر مدة كان متعة. والتحقيق ما في فتح القدير أن المتعة عقد على امرأة لا يراد به مقاصد عقد النكاح من القرار للولد وتربيته بل إما إلى مدة معينة ينتهي العقد بانتهائها أو غير معينة بمعنى بقاء العقد ما دام معها إلى أن ينصرف عنها فيدخل فيه بمادة المتعة والنكاح الموقت أيضاً فيكون من أفراد المتعة وإن عقد بلفظ التزويج وأحضر الشهود إلى آخر ما ذكره. وقد نقل في الهداية إجماع الصحابة على حرمته وأنها كانت مباحة ثم نسخت. وفي صحيح مسلم عنه كنت أذنت لكم في الاستمتاع بالنساء وقد حرم الله ذلك إلى يوم القيامة (۱) والأحاديث في ذلك كثيرة شهيرة، وما نقل عن ابن عباس من إباحتها فقد صح رجوعه وما في الهداية من نسبته إلى مالك فغلط كما ذكره الشارحون، فحينئذ كان زفر القائل بإباحة الموقت محجوجاً بالإجماع لما علمت أن الموقت من أفراد المتعة. قالوا : ثلاثة أشياء نسخت مرتين : المتعة ولحوم الحمر الأهلية والتوجه إلى بيت المقدس. أطلق في الموقت فشمل المدة الطويلة أيضاً كأن يتزوجها إلى مائتي سنة وهو ظاهر المذهب وهو الصحيح كما في المعراج، لأن التأقيت هو المعين لجهة المتعة. وشمل المدة المجهولة أيضاً. وقيد بالموقت لأنه لو تزوجها على أن يطلقها بعد شهر، فإنه جائز لأن اشتراط القاطع يدل على انعقاده مؤبداً وبطل الشرط. كما في القنية. ولو تزوجها وفي نيته أن يقعد معها مدة نواها فالنكاح صحيح لأن التوقيت إنما يكون باللفظ. قالوا: ولا بأس بتزوج النهاريات وهو أن يتزوجها ليقعد معها نهاراً دون الليل. وينبغي أن لا يكون هذا الشرط لازماً عليها ولها أن تطلب المبيت عندها ليلاً لما عرف في باب القسم.
His statement: 'and the marriage of mut'ah and muwaqqat is invalid, and the difference between them in al-Nihayah and al-Mi'raj is that in muwaqqat the word 'nikah' or 'tazwij' is mentioned along with specification of a time, while in mut'ah the wording 'I enjoy you' or 'I seek enjoyment' is used. In al-Inayah there is another difference: muwaqqat occurs in the presence of witnesses and a specific time is mentioned, unlike mut'ah because if he says 'I enjoy you' without mentioning a time, it is mut'ah. The verification is in Fath al-Qadir: mut'ah is a contract with a woman which does not aim at the objectives of marriage such as establishing lineage and raising children, but either for a specific time after which the contract ends, or for an unspecified time meaning the contract continues as long as he stays with her until he leaves her. Thus, it includes what is expressed with the root of enjoyment (mut'ah) and also time-limited marriage, so muwaqqat is a subset of mut'ah, even if contracted with the word 'tazwij' and with witnesses, to the end of what he mentioned. It is reported in al-Hidayah that there is consensus of the Companions on its prohibition, and that it was initially permissible and then abrogated. In Sahih Muslim: 'I had permitted you enjoyment of women, and Allah has prohibited that until the Day of Judgment.' The hadiths on this are numerous and well-known. The report from Ibn Abbas permitting it is established that he retracted. The attribution in al-Hidayah to Malik is a mistake, as the commentators mentioned. Thus, Zafar who says muwaqqat is permissible is defeated by consensus because it is known that muwaqqat is a subset of mut'ah. They said: Three things were abrogated twice: mut'ah, the meat of domestic donkeys, and facing Jerusalem (in prayer). The ruling (in the text) is absolute regarding muwaqqat, so it includes even a long period, such as marrying a woman for two hundred years, and this is the apparent madhhab and the correct view as in al-Mi'raj, because specifying a time defines the nature of mut'ah. It also includes an unknown period. It is restricted to time-bound marriage because if he marries her with the condition that he will divorce her after a month, it is valid because the stipulation of a terminating condition indicates that the contract is permanent and the condition is void, as in al-Qunyah. If he marries her with the intention of staying with her for a period he intends, the marriage is valid because time-binding is only by explicit wording. They said: There is no harm in marrying 'daytime women', which is that he marries a woman to stay with her during the day and not at night. It should be that this condition is not binding on her, and she has the right to request spending the night with her, as is known in the section on division of nights.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that both mut'ah (temporary marriage) and muwaqqat (time-bound marriage) are invalid and prohibited. The commentator first distinguishes between them based on wording and presentation: muwaqqat uses the words 'nikah' or 'tazwij' along with a specified time and witnesses, while mut'ah uses 'tamattu' or 'istimta''. However, the verification from Fath al-Qadir is that muwaqqat is essentially a subset of mut'ah because both involve a contract that does not intend permanence or the objectives of marriage like lineage and child-rearing. The evidence for prohibition is strong: ijma' (consensus) of the Companions, the hadith in Sahih Muslim where the Prophet (pbuh) says Allah has prohibited it until the Day of Judgment, and numerous other narrations. The passage also mentions that Ibn Abbas's earlier permissive view was retracted, and an attribution to Malik in al-Hidayah is a mistake. Zafar's opinion permitting muwaqqat is refuted by consensus. Subsidiary rulings: the prohibition covers both long and unknown durations; if a condition to divorce after a month is attached, the marriage is valid and the condition void; intention alone does not make a marriage time-bound; and 'daytime marriages' (al-nahariyyat) are permissible but the wife may request the husband stay overnight as per the rules of division (qasm).

Key Terms
المتعة (al-mut'ah): A temporary marriage contract for a specified or unspecified period, not intended for permanence or procreation.
الموقت (al-muwaqqat): A marriage contract that explicitly mentions a time limit, using the words 'nikah' or 'tazwij' with witnesses and a set period.
الإجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions (or scholars) as a source of law; here, it is the consensus that mut'ah is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence cited in the passage for the prohibition of mut'ah?
A) A Quranic verse from Surah al-Nisa
B) Consensus of the Companions (ijma') and the hadith in Sahih Muslim
C) The opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa
D) An analogy to forbidden contracts

Key Takeaway
Mut'ah and muwaqqat are invalid because they contradict the permanent nature of marriage, as established by ijma' of the Companions and express prophetic prohibition.
